Preface
This Masters Thesis was conducted in collaboration with FOSS and the Department of Man-
agement Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). All requirements set to
obtain an Industrial Engineering and Management degree within the Master of Science are ful-
ﬁlled. The time period protracted from March to September 2020 with a workload equivalent
to 32.5 ECTS credits.
I, Jack Frain, conﬁrm that all information obtained and used throughout this Thesis is within
all DTU standards under their ethical conduct and hereby declare that:

work

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This Thesis investigates project delays in a globally leading organisation, focusing on ﬁnding
speciﬁc root-causes using a risk analysis.
Risk and risk management are generally deﬁned within the Thesis. Based on speciﬁc, searchable
terms, 67 articles were described and utilised throughout the literature review.
7 interviews and a workshop were analysed with ﬁnding a root-cause for project delays in mind.
The root-cause analysis showed that there were numerous reasons for project delays within the
organisation. Only 3 were chosen within this Thesis due to time constraints.
To solve the 3 root-causes, a proposed solution consisted of using a quantitative methodology
of Delphi questions merged with a red, amber, green (1 - 10) approach. The proposed solution
aimed at reducing the project delays within the Stage Gate Model currently used within the
organisation.
Essential stakeholders and representatives from the R&D sector showed an interest in using
the proposed solution in future projects with ﬁnal recommendations given after the Thesis is
handed in. As a result, testing and a rigidly deﬁned implementation plan has been left to FOSS
and future researchers. As a result, the suggested implementation plan is to have a stakeholder
workshop where projects beginning in 2021 can adopt the proposed solution. The solution can
be used as an output for multiple gates to follow in the same methodological approach.
Keywords: Quantitative methodology, Identifying Root Causes, Project Delays, Risk Manage-
ment, Innovative Solution

1
Introduction
This chapter will brieﬂy introduce the motivation and the context for this thesis (Section 1.1).
The company is then described in Section 1.2. The scope of the Thesis can then be shown on
Section 1.3. The research goals and problem formulation are presented in Section 1.3.1 and
1.3.2. The ﬁnal section is the structure of the Thesis (Section 1.4).
1.1
Motivation and Context
For organisations, innovation is crucial but presents challenges; especially in the Research and
Development (R&D) sector to remain structured and ﬂexible whilst being eﬃcient and controlled
(Dillerup, Kappler, & Oster, 2018). Increasingly, maintaining ﬂexibility ensures a methodologi-
cal approach but allows the core innovation technology to thrive (Alessi et al., 2015). However,
there is a thin line between managing highly innovative teams and ensuring work is on schedule,
below budget and with the high quality required. This was especially felt at FOSS, which will
be described in more detail within Section 4.1.
Innovation will be deﬁned in this Thesis as: a creation of ideas, thoughts or methods that meet
market demand, customer requirements or an organisational needs through a technical imple-
mentation.
The overall focus of this Thesis will be managing the innovation presently at FOSS via an analysis
and reducing project delays from the ﬁndings. This is conducted via multiple interviews and a
workshop.
1.2
Company Description
FOSS are world leading suppliers of analytical solutions for the food and agricultural industry
with over 70 years experience. Creating end-to-end solutions which improve safety, quality and
sustainability of food products. Their goal is to reﬁne measurement instruments to run intelli-
gent driven productions for companies to produce less waste and more yield. Their production,
innovation centre and headquarters are in Hillerød, Denmark. Along with creating products,
FOSS also operates as a service organisation in 28 countries with over 300 dedicated engineers.
FOSS are a well-known company in regards to innovation as their products are one-of-a-kind.
FOSS have started working alongside Danish universities to strengthen existing relationships
within the ﬁeld of research, development and education. The aim is to build up capacity and
competencies both at FOSS and university educations, and to develop leading edge science,
technology and innovation (FOSS, 2019).
1
==PAGE:12==
1.3
Scope of the Thesis
The scope of this Thesis is risk management techniques in the R&D sector at FOSS. The focus
is on developing a quantitative tool which can be used within the R&D to reduce project delays
whilst maintaining a high level of innovation.
As a member of R&D, it is understood that
projects have high demands both from customers and project managers alike. Creating a risk
management tool aims at avoiding these circumstances. The research will include a literature
review on project delays and how it can be avoided, alongside risk management deﬁnitions and
the Stage Gate Model. The objective will be to create an analysis of FOSS whilst developing a
solution for root-cause(s) of project delays.
To show the scope in terms of the departments at FOSS, ﬁgure 1 was created. This is a
condensed version of the Systems Demarcation at FOSS. The full demarcation can be seen on
ﬁgure 45 in Section 9.1 within the Appendix.
Figure 1: Reduced ﬁgure of the Systems Demarcation at FOSS
The methodology of Systems Demarcation and how it is used can be seen on Section 2.2.1.
1.3.1
Research Goals
The focus of the Thesis is to analyse FOSS through interviews and a workshop to ﬁnd root-
cause(s) for project delays.
As of now, FOSS have multiple problems related to production quality and management gov-
ernance.
Based on meetings with leading stakeholders, myself along with other researchers
identiﬁed complicated risks associated to their current initiatives (Described in detail within
Section 4.2). With limited stakeholders practising risk management, FOSS strives to utilise
their facilities with the highest possible eﬃciency whilst maintaining their areas of interest such
as eﬃcient operations, outstanding customer satisfaction and competitiveness.
2
==PAGE:13==
In order to address encountered risks within the initiatives, the goals of the thesis were created:
1. Present a coherent approach to analyse FOSS in terms of tools and techniques in the
methodology
2. Examine literature for project delays within innovative organisations
3. Use interviews and conduct a workshop to ﬁnd root-cause(s) of project delays
4. Analyse the relation of project delays to FOSS and how these can be solved
5. Discuss the solution in terms of literature and the limitations
1.3.2
Problem Statement
The Problem Statement (Also known as the Research Question) is shown below:
How can a risk analysis be used to reduce project delays in an innovative organisa-
tion?
For the problem statement to be answered, a set of sub-questions will be elaborated on through-
out the report:
1. What tools have been executed by FOSS to eliminate the risk of not completing project
milestones?
2. How have these tools been integrated into FOSS to reduce project delays?
3. What are the supplementary tools that can be implemented and integrated to reduce the
risk of project delays at FOSS - and what are the beneﬁts/limitations to these?
1.4
Structure of Thesis
The structure of the Thesis does not follow a strict methodology but rather a process improve-
ment approach. This provides a higher level of clarity for each section. It aims to improve
readers understanding of each area with a simple deﬁnition of the chapter prior after the title.
On chapter 2, the methodology for each tool and technique is described. This includes each
tool that FOSS currently uses and the tools which are used throughout the Thesis. Within this,

2
Methodology
The methodology section aims at discussing the methods used throughout the Thesis. It begins
with an overview to how the literature was selected and why (Section 2.1).
Thereafter, the
tools are represented with alternatives and limitations described where applicable (Section 2.2).
Finally, an overview of which data was collected and how this will be analysed throughout the
Thesis is shown (Section 2.3).
2.1
Literature Review Methodology
The literature review methodology looks into how literature is selected and assessed. Once this
has been completed, it can be analysed and written within the Literature Review (Section 3).
Within this Thesis, a scoping review will be conducted. This provides an indication of which
elements will emerge within the Thesis. Alongside this, it undertakes a systematic approach
to identify gaps within research which implicates how the practise and research will be used
in future papers. There are ﬁve generic steps that are used within this Thesis to conduct the
literature review:
1. Formulating a Problem Formulation and Statement
2. Searching the Extent of Literature
3. Screening for Inclusion
4. Assessing the Quality of the Literature
5. Analysing and Writing Information
The ﬁrst step is to formulate a problem statement and questions. This is conducted with the
relevant stakeholders associated within this project. This was conducted and described in Sec-
tion 1.3.
The second area included using speciﬁc search words. This was completed systematically but
also through discussions with the supervisor and FOSS stakeholders alike. This resulted in 4
sets being created with important keywords in each. Figures 3 and 4 shows the ﬁrst, second,
third and fourth set of keywords:
5
==PAGE:16==
Figure 3: The ﬁrst- and second set of keywords are listed. The * is a search query which allows
the bending of all possible terms of the word. For instance ’Project Delays’ also considers ’Project
Delay’.
Figure 4: The third- and fourth set of keywords are listed. The * is a search query which allows
the bending of all possible terms of the word. For instance ’Change Management’ also considers
’Change Manage’
The keywords were structured in a SCOPUS search query which searched in titles, abstracts,
and keywords of articles at the ﬁnished publication stage in journals, written only in English.
The literature search resulted in 358 articles of interest. The articles were then administrated
and managed in the third step.
The third step evaluates the applicability of the material identiﬁed in regards to the problem
statement. This requires a signiﬁcant amount of reﬂective time spent on determining whether
articles, papers and general information should be provided. This must ensure that objectivity,
biases and mistakes are avoided or certain reviews will lead into an incorrect conclusion and
scope. This led to 89 articles that could be extracted systematically.
The fourth step is to assess the quality of the literature. Alongside ﬁnding screening for the
correct literature in the review, one must assess the scientiﬁc quality of the selected studies.
This uses multiple sources of literature such as journals, papers, dissertations, etc., and com-
pares these to verify the quality. It is important to collectively verify whether the source is
either quantitatively or qualitatively analysed. Articles were on continuously classiﬁed into nine
categories, and could be categorised by several at the same time: Innovation; Project Delays;
Risk Management Techniques; Performance; Adaptability; Project Gate Reviews; Qualitative
6
==PAGE:17==
Tools; Quantitative Tools; Voice of Customer. They were the assessed on an integer scale of
0 to 2 for the quality compared to the problem formulation. Where 0 was not relevant, 1 was
partly relevant and 2 was highly relevant. Articles scoring 1 were reviewed a second time, more
in depth to decide the quality and relevance. This left 67 articles which the literature review is
based on.
The ﬁnal is to synthesise, analyse and write the information. Within this step, one must sum-
marise, aggregate, organise and compare the literature extracted from the included studies. The
literature written must be meaningful and suggest the extent to which the contributor went
too. Furthermore, meta-analysis of the qualitative studies will give light of several tools and
techniques within the ﬁeld.
It is important to recognise the limitations of using this type of literature review methodology:
−Using only SCOPUS, the supervisor and FOSS as a database for peer-reviewed articles
−Restricted to key-words only
−Does not include all books or similar material
2.2
Tool Methodology
A wide variety of tools are used within this report. These tools were critically assessed both to
align with FOSS stakeholders and to create a coherent report. Why these tools were selected
was to have an easy-to-read Thesis for the stakeholders whilst being critical of tools that FOSS
have previously used. Alternative tools and limitations are mentioned within this section or
once the tool is used within the Thesis. In order to understand how the tools are conducted,
the following sections will give an overview. Furthermore, where the tools are used within the
Thesis and why the tools are typically conducted is described. This section should be a reference
for the entire report and will be quoted.
2.2.1
Systems Demarcation Methodology
Within risk management, a development of a cause/eﬀect diagram establishes a development of
the correct system in the correct way. Before this can be established, it is essential to map out the
scope of the analysis via a stakeholder mapping. The System Demarcation sets all stakeholders
in a hierarchical system who are relevant within the internal or external organisation with their
departments (Diefenbach & Sillince, 2011). From here, the system can scope in terms of the
area of solution, the area of intervention, the area and the environment. The area of solution is
a speciﬁc department or task which the solution aims at developing and mitigating risks. The
area of intervention covers the system area which are relevant for the area of solution. The area
of impact/eﬀect is normally a larger area which the solution can and possibly will eﬀect with a
range of risk mitigating solutions. Finally, the environment is the real world outside the deﬁned
7
==PAGE:18==
system. The two advantages of using this system is that all results can be documented in terms
of a department and area of impact/eﬀect. And secondly, the relationship between stakeholders
in regard to their departments and tasks is visually represented. The tool is used within Section
1.3. Figure 5 represents a blank version of a Systems Demarcation.
Figure 5: Blank Systems Demarcation
2.2.2
Coopers Stage Gate Model Methodology
In highly innovative companies, this Coopers Stage Gate Model is crucial for stakeholders to
focus on the projects timeline.
More speciﬁcally, when companies want to develop process,
improve products or change decisions (Cooper, 2002). It is used when large projects need to be
divided over several stages with multiple stakeholders involved. Much like the Waterfall method
states, once one stage is completed, the organisation can pass through a gate and the next stage
can be started (Mulder, 2018). How the Stage Gate Model is used within this Thesis is shown
within Section 4.3. In the traditional method, there are 6 stages (Including stage 0) and 5 gates
which can be seen in ﬁgure 6.
Figure 6: Coopers Traditional Stage Gate Model
8
==PAGE:19==
Stage 0 - Discovery - The ﬁrst step is brainstorming within the company in order to generate
suggested ideas. All relevant stakeholders including customers should be included. An idea is
selected and proposed. If it agreed upon then this gate will open, if not, this gate closes here.
Stage 1 - Scoping - Following an idea, the product or idea will be discussed in regard to its
strengths and weaknesses. Based on this, opportunities and threats (in the market if relevant)
can be represented. In general, the higher the threat, the greater the chance of the gate closing.
Stage 2 - Business Plan Concept - This stage looks into the concept development prior to creat-
ing or launching the original idea. There are four steps to complete stage 2: Product deﬁnition
and analysis; Creating the business plan; Creating the project plan; and a Feasibility review.
Stage 3 - Development - This leads onto the prototyping and development of the solution. Gen-
erally, a timeline and implementation plan is created here for the company to follow. This gate
will remain closed until a ﬁnal product is created.
Stage 4 - Testing and Validation - This stage includes using the customers to validate the ﬁnal
product developed in the prior stage. Three steps are used to complete and ﬁnish this gate:
Near testing, Field testing and Market testing.
Stage 5 - Launch and Implementation - The ﬁnal stage includes a marketing strategy which leads
to the product being released. This includes essential advertisement, interviews and creating a
hype. A sales team can then estimate the total demand and distribution channels.
The limitations of Coopers Stage Gate Model methodology must be listed:
−Upfront planning takes a large amount of time
−Customers are often not included until late in the Stage Gate Model
−Stakeholder inputs and outputs are lacking
The largest limitation to the Stage Gate Model is the upfront time committed to handling
projects. The literature review is used to describe gates in terms of innovation within Section
3.4. Alternative methodologies are looked into during the literature review and include the Agile
and Lean Methodologies 3.3.1.
2.2.3
RASCI Methodology
The RASCI project management technique utilises a wide range of stakeholders to hand re-
sponsibilities out. Usually applied at the start of a project to clarify which employees should
take concerns or questions too (Elhady & Abushama, 2015). It is an eﬀective methodology for
documentation of projects and to ensure that stakeholders are informed for every step of the
way. How it is used within the Thesis is shown within Section 4.4.2. Figure 7 was created to
better aid which stakeholder is chosen at which step of RASCI.
9
==PAGE:20==
Figure 7: RASCI Methodology Acronyms Explained
The limitations of RASCI methodology are listed once it is described in relation to the current
situation at FOSS (Section 4.4.2).
Because this is a tool that FOSS use in their Level 2 template (Section 4.4), alternative method-
ologies will not be looked into.
2.2.4
SIPOC Methodology
Supplier, Input, Process, Output, Customer (SIPOC) is a tool that illustrates a high-end eﬀective
level process map. The concept is that each individual concept of the SIPOC is viewed as a
diﬀerent process and will provide clarity to all stakeholders. The SIPOC can be a reference
point for stakeholders to understand and explain a project (Mishra & Sharma, 2014). How
the SIPOC is used within the Thesis is seen on Section 4.4.3. Furthermore, problems can be
identiﬁed and isolated to ﬁnd redundant areas.
To understand the SIPOC acronym, the 5
processes are explained below:
Supplier - The provider of the input to the process.
Input - All information and resources needed to complete the overall process.
Process - A number of chronologically ordered tasks to complete the input and turn it into an
output.
Output - End result of the process - Could also include a new input process to start over.
Customer - Recipient of the output.
The limitations of SIPOC methodology are listed once it is described in relation to the current
situation at FOSS (Section 4.4.3).
10
==PAGE:21==
Again, because this is a tool that FOSS use in their Level 2 template (Section 4.4), alternative
methodologies will not be looked into.
2.2.5
Relationship Matrix Diagram Methodology
This simple tool is used to visualise how two or more elements correlate to each other in terms
of a diagram (Burge, 2011). Henceforth, it allows for a comprehensive and eﬀortless correlation
of information. Because relationships are more complex than one-to-one and often require more
than one direction of thinking (many-to-many), this matrix allows a complicated relationship
to be analysed in a straight forward manner. This is used as the basis of the analysis section
(Section 5.3.1). Figure 8 shows the blank version of the relationship matrix diagram.
Figure 8: Blank Relationship Matrix Diagram
As seen on ﬁgure 8, on the horizontal headline, a date, action or project is put in place. On
the vertical headlines, a name or terminology is placed. In the main body of the matrix, there
are four possible options to correlate the headlines. The ﬁrst is leaving it blank which means
there is no correlation, the second is a weak correlation (+1), denoted as a triangle, the third is a
11
==PAGE:22==
blank circle, which is a medium correlation (+3), fourthly is a strong correlation (+9), denoted
as a ﬁlled in circle. The numbers are used to total up on the ﬁnal horizontal line to ﬁnd which
area has the largest correlation.
The limitations of Relationship Matrix Diagram Methodology can be seen on Section 5.3.1.
Alternative relationship correlation tools can be used such as a statistical pattern.
This is
however dismissed as it may be too complicated for some stakeholders to understand. Another
alternative is to use a qualitative approach from interviews to match the relationship. These
patterns are hard to justify so the relationship matrix diagram was a better ﬁt.
2.2.6
Ishikawa Diagram
The Ishikawa Diagram (Figure 9) is an essential tool in used in a root-cause analysis.
For
industrial applications, the ‘8 M’s’ are generally used: ‘Management’, ‘Measurement’, ‘Material’,
‘Machines’, ‘Maintenance’ , ‘Mother Nature (Environment)’, ‘Man Power’ and ‘Method’ (Liliana,
2016). Sub-causes are collected under the categories which have a common eﬀects. The Ishikawa
diagram is normally used with a secondary root-cause analysis tool to ﬁnd one problem. This
tool is used within the Thesis on Section 5.3.2. Figure 9 represents the blank generic Ishikawa
diagram.
Figure 9: Blank Ishikawa Diagram
The Ishikawa Diagram can be used alongside the 5 Why’s or as a stand alone tool. Alternative
tools could be the mind map or a SWOT analysis of an organisation. The mind map can result
in an unstructured development of root-causes and the SWOT analyses too many external fac-
12
==PAGE:23==
tors. The Ishikawa Diagram was used as this was familiar with FOSS stakeholders and can help
facilitate workshops.
The limitations to using the Ishikawa are described on Section 5.3.2.
2.2.7
Gravity, Urgency, Tendency (GUT) Matrix Methodology
Gravity, Urgency, Tendency (GUT) methodology is used to bring alignment and depth to the im-
pediments of another root-cause analysis (Braga, Brand˜ao, Ribeiro, & Di´ogenes, 2019). Hence-
forth, create a prioritisation of which root-cause should be looked into accordingly. It is simple
but the quantitative decisions need to be justiﬁed. The tool is used in this Thesis within the
root-cause analysis on Section 5.3.3. It is essential within this GUT Matrix to deﬁne how each
element was analysed and described. Each topic should be taken from a separate root-cause
tool and then be subject to being analysed against the gravity, urgency and tendency of the or-
ganisation. The terms are described below. The numbers 1 to 5 are deﬁned using the list below.
The original use of the GUT was within Brazilian architecture (?, ?) so the same deﬁnitions
were used within this Thesis.
Gravity
If a problem is not solved, then the gravity addresses the intensity and impact the problem can
cause. Also known as severity, the score varies from 1 to 5 and is described as follows:
1. Not severe
2. Little severe
3. Severe
4. Very much severe
5. Extremely severe
Urgency
The time pressure associated with ﬁnding s solution to a problem. The urgency is scored from
1 to 5 with and described as follows:
1. It can wait
2. Not very urgent
3. Urgent and deserves short term attention
4. Very urgent
5. Immediate action to be taken
Tendency
The pattern or trend of the situation. The potential growth of a problem and the probability
of this problem becoming worse over time. Again, a 1 to 5 scale is used:
1. Will not change
2. Will worsen in the long run
13
==PAGE:24==
3. Will worsen in the medium run
4. Will worsen in the shirt run
5. Will worsen immediately
G * U * T
These numbers can be placed in a table and multiplied to have the highest result correspondent.
Therefore, the most critical problem to solve. A blank table was created to better understand
how the GUT will be used. Figure 10 shows this:
Figure 10: Blank GUT Table
Alternative tools were looked into such as the Pareto Chart to analyse the frequency of prob-
lems occurring. This was disregarded because it only shows the frequency of a problem, not
the severity or urgency. Another tool could have been the Failure Mode and Eﬀects Analysis
(FMEA). This is dismissed because it has already been completed numerous times at FOSS.
This might lead to a strong bias of severity in the root-cause analysis.
The limitations to using the GUT are described on Section 5.3.3.
2.3
Data Methodology
There are multiple ways of conducting a data analysis, but within risk management, qualitative
information is the norm. The entire data methodology follows a methodological triangulation.
This means that within this Thesis, not only one qualitative technique is looked into but rather
a combination of two or more methods. In order to analyse the triangulation, an analysis was
chosen which is presented thereafter.
2.3.1
Methodological Triangulation
A research can be highly bias if only one methodological qualitative or quantitative technique
is used. Henceforth, triangulation oﬀ-sets the weaknesses and aims to validate and improve
the reliability of data. In this Thesis, a Between-method triangulation will be upheld, meaning
that contrasting data collection methods will be examined and compared using the qualitative
methodology (Denzin, 1970). Figure 11 below shows an example of 3 separate data collection
methods:
14
==PAGE:25==
Figure 11: An Example of Triangulation with 3 Common Data Collection Methods
The main advantages of conducting triangulation in this manner are listed below:
−Compensation of shortcomings from one data collection method
−A holistic view of the behaviour and complete
−Accuracy, reliability and truth can be contrasted and compared under the same or diﬀerent
circumstances - allowing researchers to ﬁnd diﬀerences
During triangulation can the researcher examine multiple stakeholders under diﬀerent circum-
stances to understand complications. This however leads to some limitations such as longer
time and eﬀort at the expense of the researcher; understanding contradictory statements from
reliable sources; avoiding generalisation of qualitative methods being unreliable. This is used
within the Thesis in the analysis on Section 5.
Even with triangulation, the sources used must be examined. The data overview for this Thesis
can be seen on ﬁgure 12:

2.3.3
How to Analyse Qualitative Data
To analyse the qualitative data collected, it is important to deﬁne the context. Conducting a
qualitative analysis means looking at the context of a situation and examining the language used
in interviews, conversations or comments (ICC). The idea of the analysis, is not on individual
words or sounds but to study larger portions of language. Therefore, the selected sources can
be analysed on multiple levels. There are a number of terms that need to be introduced. These
terms within linguistics are used to analyse how truth worthy the stakeholders are, and whether
they seem to be contradicting other statements. The six terms can be seen in ﬁgure 13 below:
Figure 13: Qualitative Language Deﬁnitions
There are 4 steps that are needed whilst conducting a qualitative analysis. These were created
into ﬁgure 14 below for simplicity:
Figure 14: 4 Steps of Conducting a Qualitative Analysis
16
==PAGE:27==
The four steps can then be elaborated in order to have a better understanding of how these can
be used in an analysis.
Step 1 is to deﬁne the context in which the interviews were conducted. If they were not your
own ICC then this is essential to understand linguistically. If this is incorrectly used, then wrong
assumptions can be easily created.
Step 2 is gathering information. One must establish and understand within the ICC, why they
might be implying certain aspects. Factual information must be used to the real-life content of
the information.
Step 3 is to analyse the content. Once the context has been set and information has been
gathered, analysing various elements of the material related to your problem formulation is per-
formed. This includes attributing answers from the ICC to the deﬁned communication levels in
ﬁgure 13.
Step 4 is to review the results and draw conclusions from them. This is not just related to
your own topic but also a wider range of research for the future. Are questions missing, is the
content too broad or are their unreliable stakeholders.
From the methodology, the literature review can begin.
This uses the described literature
methodology (Section 2.1).

==PAGE:28==
3
Literature Review
The literature review gives an ideal perspective from the multiple points of view on the main topics
surrounding this Thesis. It consists of four state-of-the-art topics which will be described in-depth
and then a ﬁnal missing literature links which establishes a gap in the literature. The literature
was taken from 67 articles and papers (After scoping from the Methodology (Section 2.1). Also
described on the Literature Review Methodology, ten keywords were selected. From these, four
sections were researched: Project Delays and How They Can be Avoided; Risk Management;
Challenges Facing Innovative Organisations; Project Gate Reviews.
3.1
Project Delays and How They Can be Avoided
This Thesis revolves around project delays within an innovative organisation. Delays can occur
for a number of reasons. This section will look into how to avoid these delays.
Organisations need to remain competitive in both their productivity and eﬃciency, thus it is
increasingly important to acknowledge the diﬃculties and uncertainties that can arise (Crespi
& Zuniga, 2011) throughout a project. Although organisations tend to beneﬁt from multiple
stakeholder collaboration to avoid project delays, it is essential that new knowledge is individ-
ually pragmatically established (Savage et al., 2010). This means that new tools an techniques
must be understood individually before attempting to collaborate with numerous stakeholders.
After the analysis of multiple innovation literature, ﬁve criteria eﬀect the success or failure
of project timelines (?, ?):
1. Internal Communication
2. Use of technology
3. Key stakeholder inﬂuences
4. Market Endorsement
5. Quality of products
Internal communication
This is a central theme amongst researchers as this area is crucial in low-power distance counties
(Daniels & Greguras, 2014) such as Scandinavia. In order to maintain a project timeline, internal
communication should lead employees through contrasting phases of projects (Linke & Zerfass,
2011). This internal communication was primarily through staﬀmeetings and workshops because
of the high level of stakeholder interaction. This, according to the employees of this company,
created a high level of awareness, understanding and acceptance.
This transparent internal
communication lead to a statistic being created which depicts the frequencies of innovation
ideas and how they arise within the company. Through a chi-squared test, the responses were
checked for how randomly the answer distribution fell. From this, a conclusion was related to
their original hypothesis that project timelines improved, the better the internal communication
(Linke & Zerfass, 2011).
18
==PAGE:29==
Contrasting to this theory, the wheel of leaderships is a methodology represents how internal
communication can be utilised throughout a project to minimise project delays through four key
aspects (Zerfass & Huck, 2007): Social dimensions, Cognitive dimensions, Eﬀective dimensions
and Conative dimensions. If a project manager can utilise these 4 areas then internal commu-
nication can be improved throughout a project team. This framework was built on the prior
knowledge of three levels of communication. The ﬁrst level is the macro level; where new ideas
and technologies enable large communication platforms (such as media and articles) to solve a
speciﬁc challenge which generally inﬂuences a wide variety of people. The second level is on a
meso level; this is an organisational level which creates an understanding of marketing, public
relations and internal platforms. The third level is the micro level; where managers can inﬂuence
the project timeline by personal relationships amongst multiple organisations (Eslinger, Moll, &
de Oliveira-Souza, 2002). Whilst creating a tool for an organisation, it is essential to recognise
the internal communication and how the platform will hinder or improve this.
Use of technology
This plays a pivotal role in society and organisations alike. At an organisational level, technical
innovation enhances an increased production and quality level, whilst maintaining the original
project timeline (Diaconu, 2011), within their R&D sector. Studies which have oriented around
the input costs within the R&D conclude that ﬁrms themselves do not innovate but a continuous
improvement between the environment (such as Governments and customers) and the employees
in the organisation lead to technological innovations.
This technical innovation, instead of
costing extra time for the organisation, will reduce and improve the project timeline (Tohidi &
Jabbari, 2012).
Furthermore, a detailed report (Paunov & Planes-Satorra, 2019) reviews evidence from agri-
culture, automotive and retail articles to ﬁnd how the recent digital technologies are changing
innovation and how this eﬀects project timelines. Five trends are found that eﬀect the use of
technology across all industries: Innovation is increasingly data-based; services are at the centre
of innovation; innovation cycles are accelerating; innovation processes are more collaborative;
and ﬁrms invest in new organisational capabilities to better embrace digital innovation. These
ﬁve ﬁndings are the preliminary understanding point for technology oriented organisation ap-
proaches. It is concluded within multiple literature that one way to organise these characteristics
is via a sectoral road-map or strategic foresight brainstorm (Minhas, Ahmed, Siddiq, Nadeem,
& Gabol, 2011) prior to each project beginning. Thus, boosting the ability to adapt to the
innovative needs of a singular project and investing highly on the R&D within this adopted
innovative environment.
Key stakeholder inﬂuences
It is widely known that stakeholders such as managers are at the forefront of decisions on
projects, programmes or portfolios. Thus, it comes as little surprise that these stakeholders
impact project timelines through their leadership. One source argues that secondary stakehold-
19
==PAGE:30==
ers (those who do not directly impact an organisation) can be more inﬂuential on innovation
than the primary stakeholders (Goodman, Korsunova, & Halme, 2017).
Thus, these stake-
holders should be more involved within the project development than previously considered.
Secondary stakeholders include customers and direct business competitors. For the primary
stakeholders though, employees according to (Eslinger et al., 2002) need to feel that they work
in an innovative environment and should have this as part of the companies mission statement.
Key stakeholders therefore have to create an innovation environment that employees are con-
tent and satisﬁed with to reduce project delays. This is also reﬂected within choosing diverse
management to conduct ground up strategies. Before managing this, it is advised to create a
stakeholder identiﬁcation and stakeholder analysis as a basic instrument (Maj, 2015). This al-
lows a collaboration between the particular organisation and any related stakeholders which can
inﬂuence the innovation scheme. In relation to the key stakeholder inﬂuences, these stakeholders
must look into the market capabilities of their production, innovation and product development.
Market Endorsement
An eﬀective tool to enable a larger customer base whilst enhancing project delays, is market
endorsement. The most successful market endorsements ensure that their return on investments
(ROI) is higher than the input cost. To measure this, an organisation must develop multiple
Key Point Indicators (KPIs) which are independent from one another (Costello, 1985). One of
the major ﬁndings within the same report, is that new members of an organisation should be
fully informed of how market endorsements are performed. This allows for a transparent and an
easy-to-follow guideline of the businesses way to perform. Not to memorize a 700 page manual.
This explicit way of documenting innovations, speciﬁcally aimed towards the Business Model
of Innovation (Cortes, 2014). By optimising the market endorsement for new employees, it is
argued that project delays will be minimised. Within this, products are categorised as one of
four technologies: radical, semi-radical, incremental, semi-incremental. To be critical of this pa-
per, one organisation rarely can stick to one category only and remain competitive. Innovative
organisations tend to aim for radical products which transform information or products within
the current market. In order to manage this market endorsement, (McDermott & O’Connor,
2002) developed a strategic approach utilising existing markets and how radical products eﬀect
market resistance.
Quality of products
One must ensure that the quality of products corresponds to the innovative culture an organ-
isation creates (Ceausu, Murswieck, Kurth, & Ionescu, 2017). This is because customers are
either quality-oriented or price-oriented. For innovative products, it is mainly based on quality-
oriented since they are distinctive (Ramos, 2013). To further this, it is argued that just simply
cost-cutting innovative products is redundant and long term trends should seek after that impact
the ﬁrm. One of these impacts is benchmarking competitors to learn their innovative cultures
and bettering their practises. Another is proactively focusing on the customers by initiating,
20
==PAGE:31==
identifying, communicating and responding to their demands. One limitation of this work that
has been noted is that it is based on large enterprises who do not necessarily rely on a large cash
ﬂow from one innovative product. Thus, research was conducted on Small-Medium Enterprises
(SME’s) to establish an unbiased investigation. The main focus of SME’s was to improve their
technique for creating innovative products (Hanif & Manarvi, 2009). Therefore, reaching the
market in innovative ways even if there is an economic crisis.
These 5 elements which have been reviewed need to have a success criteria which if reached,
gives the company an ability to reduce project delays. All elements come from diﬀerent sources
and should be reﬂected on throughout the Thesis. To manage these, risk management deﬁnitions
are introduced in the next section.
3.2
Risk Management
Risk is deﬁned in terms of an uncertain event which may have a positive or negative eﬀect on
a projects objectives (Ramos, 2013). Risk management is a planned and structured approach
which aims at identifying, classifying and quantifying risks and then controlling them.
3.2.1
Risk Management Deﬁnitions
There are many sources of risk within a project which could potentially lead to negative risks
and elongated project timelines. These risks are mentioned below as 11 aspects:
−Strategic risk: Implementation of the business strategy.
−Operations risk: A ﬁrms ability to produce and supply goods or services internally.
−Supply risk: The internal and external ﬂow of resources that enable the companies opera-
tions to run smoothly.
−Customer risk: How likely the customers are to buy from the company alongside the
companies presence in their current market.
−Asset impairment risk: How much an asset is utilised and the ability to generate an income
based on the asset.
−Competitive risk: Aﬀects a ﬁrm’s ability to diﬀerentiate its products or services from its
competitors.
−Reputation risk: Arises from a business losing conﬁdence in itself or others.
−Financial risk: The potential change in ﬁnancial markets due to a loss in products or
exposure.
−Fiscal risk: A change in taxes which eﬀects the company ﬁnancially.
−Regulatory risk: External environment (Usually Governmental) which exposes the ﬁrm to
change regulations.
−Legal risk: The ﬁrms litigation with action arising from customers, suppliers, shareholders
or employees.
21
==PAGE:32==
These deﬁnitions are essential as it sets the boundary to which risk of the Thesis will be fo-
cused. It also allows the success criteria to be set for the 5 elements of project delays: 1) Internal
Communication; 2) Use of technology; 3) Key stakeholder inﬂuences; 4) Market Endorsement;
5) Quality of products. Furthermore, multiple methodologies are described with separate risk
management applications. One of these methodologies is the identiﬁcation of risks and poten-
tially how to mitigate them. A table known as the ”Summary of inputs, tools and techniques
and outputs on risk management plan phase” is shown within this qualitative research paper
(Peixoto, Tereso, Fernandes, & Almeida, 2016). The table documents risk management reviews
and which tools should be the output of the historical situations within a company. It is therefore
possible to compare knowledge gained on one project versus a potential future project. Hence,
mitigating the same risk factors. This table can be seen on ﬁgure 15:
Figure 15: Summary of inputs, tools and techniques and outputs on risk management plan phase
(Peixoto et al., 2016)
3.2.2
How Risk Management can be used in Projects
Risk management has numerous applications in organisations. This section looks at how risk
management can be implemented and the limitations of these. Risk management applications
are intrinsically linked to decision making.
Projects have a certain amount of risk which has been deﬁned in Section 3.2.1. In order
to manage these applications, it is advised to manage and initiate plans before projects begin.
Once a risk is identiﬁed, it should be assessed and given a probability of occurring. This can be
then prioritised (high, medium, low) and managed depending on the organisations strategy. As
part of the documentation of risks, two important items must be looked into: 1) Mitigating the
risk after the identiﬁcation and 2) contingency plan once the risk occurs (Stather, 2006).
To ﬁnd how these applications can be used in risk management, a list of questions were
created by (Roberts, Wallace, & McClure, 2003):
−What speciﬁc possible outcomes do we face?
−Are these outcomes related?
−How sensitive are our strategies, cash ﬂow, earnings etc. to the occurrence of future events?
−Is our achievement of critical objectives aﬀected by future events?
22
==PAGE:33==
−How capable are we of responding to whatever may happen in the future?
−How much potential reward is required before we are willing to accept the risks associated
with the uncertainties that we face?
−If we decide to accept the exposures giving rise to our risks, do we have suﬃcient capital
to absorb signiﬁcant unforeseen losses, should they occur?
The questions are an essential qualitative methodological approach to risk management. It
allows the project team to risk proﬁle within a project. The questions will be used for the basis
of interviews and the workshop.
This is however criticised by an analysis of high investment projects (Kaczorek, Krzemi´nski,
& Ibadov, 2017). It is argued that high cost projects generate a higher need for management of
risks thought a project manager, instead of just planning for risks. Accordingly, the scope of the
projects can be occasionally incomparable. It is advised by Kaczorek er al., (2017) to not use
universal risk management approaches on projects but to specify and adapt to various inputs.
One other side of risk management is the start of management initiatives and how risk can
be mitigated externally and internally. Initiatives can be hazardous due to the implementation
time and cost. In a valuable initiative, the initiative beneﬁt will outweigh these costs. One
method known as the Industry Portfolio of Risks (?, ?) which is more commonly used to analyse
initiative uncertainty. Within this model, four common risks are described: 1) Financial risks;
2) Strategic risks; 3) Hazard risks; 4) Operational risks. Within these, the model thoroughly
describes every risk related to the four criteria if an initiative is started within an organisation.
The drawback of the model is that if a risk is found, there is no protocol to mitigate this risk,
only to obtain that there is a potential threat (Kroczak et al., 2006).
Alongside the risk management, within this Thesis, it is important to recognise the challenges
facing innovative organisations.
3.3
Challenges Facing Innovative Organisations
It is important in this Thesis, to research challenges facing innovative organisations and how
these can be overcome with methodologies. A large challenge facing innovative organisation
lies within their ability to adapt to Volatility, Uncertain, Complex and Ambiguous (VUCA)
environments (Charbonnier-Voirin & Roussel, 2012). As global mega-trends lead to an uncertain
economy, it becomes essential to acclimate to complicated and risky situations. It is argued that
the more an organisation knows, the more uncertain it seems to be (Sobratee & Bodhanya, 2018).
This is because unknown unknowns are more prevalent. Sobratee & Bodhanya argue through
intensive quantitative research, that present day risk management should be able to adapt to
a turbulent and uncertain concentrated issues by relating this to decentralisation, decision-
making styles, the use of controls, norms and values, communication or technology within the
company (As explained in Section 3.2). The limitation to the literature regarding innovation, is
the lack of crucial empirical data that is assessed. To overcome this lack of quantitative data,
23
==PAGE:34==
(Hashemi, Amezaga, Connell, & Attari, 2007) analyse a set of projects and provide critical
elusive solutions to volatile problems. It is advised that by creating a speciﬁc risk management
solution for an organisation, the gap between a managerial strategy and innovation talent can be
ﬁlled. Moreover, it allows a possible identiﬁcation of advantageous strategies that organisational
leaders increasingly can rely on (Sobratee & Bodhanya, 2018) within projects.
Within innovative organisations, it is imperative to have employees remain ﬂexible but on
schedule with deadlines (Khazanchi, Lewis, & Boyer, 2007) as explained within the ﬁrst section
of the literature review (Section 3.1). By empowering employees through managing innovation,
employees can remain ﬂexible. This can be achieved by using qualitative methodologies such as
Lean or Agile (Section 3.3.1).
This however does not justify how an organisation can adapt to complicated environments.
This can be explained through maintaining business excellence. Business excellence is a term
that is vastly used throughout industries but is also extremely ambiguous (Carvalho, Sampaio,
Rebentisch, Carvalho, & Saraiva, 2019). Depending on the industry and their management,
the deﬁnition can be juxtaposing. Business excellence can be achieved in a VUCA environment
by integrating project management with risk management technique (Hekkala, Stein, Rossi, &
Smolander, 2017). The major limitation of this is, is the current qualitative methodological
approaches, and not quantitative. In order to see the limitations of qualitative methodologies,
the following section looks into these.
3.3.1
Methodologies for Challenges Facing Innovative Organisations
One methodology and principle is known as Lean.
Lean is a misused term often used as a
synonym for Agile. The Lean methodology concerns the optimisation of a development process,
while the Lean method concerns the optimisation of a production process (?, ?). Although sim-
ilar, these terms diﬀer in multiple principles. The Lean methodology incorporates value proﬁle,
value congruence and value practices through production process eliminations. Although orig-
inating from car industries, Leans principles aid the mitigation of VUCA environments. There
are 5 principles of Lean that create a continuous cycle of improvement (Melovi´c, Mitrovi´c,
Zhuravlev, & Braila, 2016): 1) Identify Value; 2) Map the Value Stream; 3) Create Flow; 4)
Establish Pull; 5) Seek Perfection. The ﬁrst principle is deﬁning the value from a customers
perspective. The ﬁrst principle is essential for an organisation to evaluate the customers and de-
ﬁne ultimately how they perceive the most important aspect of a product. The second principle
determines how the product diﬀerentiates from similar products in the market. This is where
innovative products are essential and can penetrate the market via an individualised characteris-
tic. The third principle is to use the value stream map and analyse the individual process steps.
Therefore, replacing and minimising the redundant areas to reduce time and cost. The fourth
step again takes the customers perspectives and the question can be asked ”why the customer
speciﬁcally wants this products?”. Instead of pushing the product out, it needs to be pulled
and wanted from the customer. The ﬁfth and ﬁnal principle, is to continuously improve the
process development and get as close to perfection as possible. This concept is the umbrella for
24
==PAGE:35==
Lean and should lead back to step 1. From the Lean concept, it is clear that there are 3 key
advantages. Transitions between various designs can be shortened in terms of time. Inventory
levels can be optimised and almost nulliﬁed. And most importantly in regards to this paper,
an increased ﬂexibility and better response time to customer requirements (Strode, Huﬀ, &
Tretiakov, 2009). On the other hand, Lean manufacturing has various limitations. The ﬁrst is
that innovative companies struggle to implement Lean because it is not the sole answer to build
overall competitiveness (Chen & Taylor, 2009). The second is that Lean processes are generally
outsourced to speciﬁc manufacturing companies. Therefore, innovative companies who produce
radical products in-house struggle to strategise and implement Lean on a long term basis. Fi-
nally, in order for an organisation to decide whether to implement Lean, three conditions need
to be satisﬁed according to (Cascio & Montealegre, 2016): 1) demand is increasing dramatically;
2) Supply of knowledge from employees is increasing; 3) The company has enough connections
to technologically advancing companies to interact and aid one-another. Literature concludes
that even if all conditions are satisﬁed, an innovative, in-house producing organisation should
look into other strategies and techniques that would be more beneﬁcial (Chen & Taylor, 2009).
Another qualitative technique which was endeavoured into in terms was the Agile methodol-
ogy. The Agile methodology is an iterative approach ﬁrst used in software development. There
are six stages that align with the concepts of the Agile Manifesto created in 2001 (PMBook,
2001):
1. Planning: Once an idea is deemed viable via a brainstorm and analysis, the project team
can identify features, prioritize each feature, and assign them to an iteration.
2. Requirements analysis: Key stakeholders and customers can identify requirements and
quantify these.
3. Design: The design is taken from the requirements, and a design of solutions can be
created by deciding on a test strategy or plan to proceed.
4. Implementation or development: Developing features and scheduling iterations.
5. Testing: Test the development against the requirements created to ensure they meet the
customer needs. This phase includes unit testing, integration testing, system testing and
acceptance testing.
6. Deployment: The product can then be delivered to the customers. Once customers start
using the product, any problems can be documented need to be addressed future iterations.
The Agile methodology as explained has been researched especially within the previous
years and massively hyped up by large organisations (Atzberger et al., 2019). There are ﬁve
advantages taken from multiple resources which have been deemed the most appropriate for this
Thesis. The ﬁrst is that change is embraced within the Agile methodology, which diﬀers from
the Waterfall methodology. Therefore a re-prioritisation and stakeholders changing ideas will
be taken advantage of. Secondly, the end-goal, which is usually known in projects can be an
unknown and not clearly deﬁned. This ensures that the project is not rigid and as the project
progresses, separate goals can be reached and adapted. Thirdly and one of the most essential
25
==PAGE:36==
is customers have a large say on the end-goal development. This makes sure that instead of
completing an entire project and realising that it was not to the customers speciﬁcations or
requirements then restarting; an organisation can correctly amend deliverables. Fourthly, the
team has a high focus on a quality deliverable with a shorter deadline. This is formed through
the breakdown of a project into iterations which allows a separated development, testing and
collaboration stage. Finally, a strong team interaction allowed improved communication through
the continuous improvement methodology.
Feedback from all stakeholders is encouraged so
lessons can be taught and learnt from all previous projects.
Although ﬁve advantages have been laid out, but the Agile methodology has multiple lim-
itations. The ﬁrst disadvantage is the team has to be diversely knowledgeable. Due to the
small nature of project teams, all members must be highly skilled in a variety of ﬁelds whilst
understanding the Agile methodology. The second disadvantage is the time commitment needed
from all departments. In a Waterfall methodology, departments are separated and do not need
to attend all meetings in order to understand the requirements. However, in the Agile Method-
ology, the time consumption increases exponentially with the larger and more complex a project
becomes. Furthermore, this time is increased because of the documentation needed to complete
each step of an Agile Methodology. If the documentation is neglected then there is a ﬁne line
between balancing the innovation of an organisation and performing well. The ﬁnal limitation
is the planning can be less concrete. Once a project is started, in the Agile approach, tasks are
rescheduled in order to meet diﬀerent customer requirements. This again can add to the overall
timeline and increase the cost of a project.
One problem which needs to be described in a greater detail in regards to the Agile method-
ology, is that employees often have no prior experience (Hekkala et al., 2017). The challenging
transition from a Waterfall methodology to an Agile approach with all the challenges are docu-
mented and well described by employees by Hekkala. Amongst these, one of the largest according
to multiple sources of literature is the size of the projects and how complicated they become.
This apparently leads to an unclear understanding of Agile goals and common management
communication errors. Teams struggled to understand what the management dictated and how
they were involved in the project. From these ﬁndings, it is essential that an innovative organi-
sation must have meetings where a delegation of tasks to stakeholders within the project must
be set prior to starting. This ﬁnding will allow a more in depth analysis of the solution created
with respect to a Waterfall vs. Agile approach.
In conclusion to the Methodologies for Challenges Facing Innovative Organisations, the 2
most common qualitative techniques have been explained. These however have numerous limi-
tations in terms of adjoining them to the Project Stage Gate Model (See Section 3.4) because
it needs to simultaneously be adaptable to VUCA environments. This is extremely challenging
when an organisation is known as innovative. To ﬁnd how organisations use the Stage Gate
Model approach, the following section was created.
26
==PAGE:37==
3.4
Project Gate Reviews
With Stage Gate Models (also known as a phase-gate model) becoming normal in companies to
track changes and ensure projects are less delayed, reviewing the gates becomes more essential.
Although brieﬂy described in the methodology section (Section 2.2.2), the current literature
regarding reviewing gates needs to be considered. The staged development process is a compre-
hensive tool to ensure that at a gate, decisions are made. These decisions often have ﬁve steps;
Waiver (with and without re-review), Back-up plan, and Delay, along with Go and Kill. This is
demonstrated in ﬁgure 16 below:
Figure 16: The Decisions Made at Each Gate
Before continuing into the limitations of using this system, the deﬁnitions of each possible
scenario needs to be listed. Go is the most simple, where all intended phases have been completed
on time. Delay is when a new date is selected for the completion of a gate, and all work is
postponed until this date. Kill occurs when there is no alternative but to stop a project. Waiver
is when the project team acknowledges that part of a gate is missing but nevertheless, continues
with the next phase in the project to avoid a delay. Waiver with Re-review is an addition to
the simple waiver, where a selected date to review the acknowledged incomplete work is chosen.
Back-up plan is when a high risk project can be re-scoped to ensure the project is not delayed
but often has a less risky outcome.
With these deﬁnitions, certain insights must be known in a project such as how high risk the
technology to develop is, and if a project is killed, what is the impact within the organisation.
This leads to several complications and limitations whilst using project gate reviews. Firstly,
the stage gate model is sequential (Waterfall) and within an innovative environment, this can
lead to a rigid approach. Thus, technology advances at a slower rate. Along this same line, a
tension can exist between the organisations methodology and creative thinking of stakeholders.
Finally, within stage gate models, resource allocation is diﬃcult. The cross-functional set-up of
a typical stage gate model does not allow for dedicated employees to allocate resources, hence,
departments may have a large workload on one gate and then have no input in the rest of the
project. This could lead to documentation issues and misinformed stakeholders. According to
(Olechowski, Eppinger, & Joglekar, 2017), from 3 case studies conducted, the stage gate model
can ”represent diﬀerent estimates of conﬁdence, facilitating a discussion of heuristics, biases,
and information gaps between the decision option championed by diﬀerent functions in the
27
==PAGE:38==
organisation at the gate.” This is because project managers are more likely to accept high risk
projects and set demanding deadlines without consulting the technological expertise ﬁrst. This
leads to a high technology risk that if not quantiﬁed then the impacts revolve around killing or
delaying a project.
The stage gate however presents inherent risks. Quantitative methodologies are rare, espe-
cially in terms of passing through gates and estimating project lengths. Thus, deciding when a
gate should be delayed, killed, go, etc. is diﬃcult to decide (Cooper, 2002).
3.4.1
How Project Gates Relate with Innovation
The stage gate model is essential as it is a pillar in the Innovative Performance Framework to
drive an innovative value on a sustainable basis. Figure 17 represents the remaining pillars:
Figure 17: The Four Pillars of the Innovative Performance Framework (Mulder, 2018)
The pillars will be referred back once a solution has been developed within this Thesis within
the Discussion (Section 7.2).
The development of a methodology is a complicated initiative. An organisation must create
ideas through development of feasible approaches then scaling up into an approach that is easy
to follow. Top performing companies must therefore use the four pillars equally to successfully
ensure an alignment between the project gate and the methodology. The four pillars are based
upon 6 categories that the stage gate model must fulﬁl in order to pass according to Cooper
(Cooper, 2002):
28
==PAGE:39==
−Strategic Fit
−Product and Competitive Advantage
−Market Attractiveness
−Technical Feasibility
−Synergies/Core Competencies
−Financial Risk vs. Reward
Every project according to Cooper should be measured against six criteria which are listed
above. Each gate passed to avoid project delays should relate back to these six criteria. The
output of relating back and understanding the previous phase aids the product development and
maximises total resources used. Modern Stage Gates which incorporate these 6 criteria are said
to be:
1. Fast and ﬂexible – multiple processes exist to match project complexity and risk
2. Customer driven - a customer centric approach is built throughout the entire process
3. Dynamic and vibrant front-end – an ideation stage that is both internally and externally
focused to generate the best new ideas.
3.5
Missing Gaps in the Literature Review
To summarise the literature review, it is essential to understand the limitations of each research
attribution. Although this has been mentioned throughout the literature review, an enhanced
and scoped summary will follow in this section.
In a VUCA environment, critical quantitative methodologies are missing in risk management
techniques. This within innovative environments tends to lead to project delays in Stage Gate
Models because stakeholders are unsure when to ﬁnish
Within innovative organisations; internal communication, use of technology, key stakeholder in-
ﬂuences, market endorsement and quality of products are the largest contributors to reducing
project delays. This can be achieved, as explained by using methodologies within an organisa-
tion. The literature review identiﬁed ﬂaws in the understanding of risks and aligning these risks
with the companies current strategies and initiatives. This lead onto companies practising risk
management through a Waterfall and rigid approach called the Stage Gate Model. The Stage
Gate Model is missing a quantitative approach where risk management tools are implemented
to collaborate on speciﬁc gates. This would lead to a more comprehensive delay analysis and
how to better predict the timeline of a project. Hence, decision factors would be implemented
at the start of a gate, instead of attempting to adapt with a waiver (which is commonly used).
Although back-up plans should contain an insight on how organisations can overcome compli-
cations, it is rarely used. Even if it is used then little time has been buﬀered in to overcome
complications.
In conclusion, quantitative risk management methodologies are diﬃcult to ﬁnd and often are
not implemented with the stage gate model in mind.
