==PAGE:40==
4
Current Situation at FOSS
This section of the report introduces key elements of the current situation that will ultimately
lead onto the Analysis of the Interviews and Workshop at FOSS. It begins with an overview of the
current problem at FOSS. Thereafter, the current 5 initiatives started at FOSS to reduce project
delays and increase employee productivity are described. One initiative is chosen as described in
the Scope of the Thesis (Section 1.3. Thereafter, how initiative 5 is related to their departments
(known as streams at FOSS) is described. Lastly, the tools that FOSS have previously developed
to decrease project delays and the current tool (developed in initiative 5) are analysed.
4.1
The Magnitude of the Problem at FOSS
It is important to understand the problem which was addressed to the researcher in the The-
sis. Whilst visiting FOSS, a vast amount of problems were apparent which included quality of
products, project leadership issues, missing customer speciﬁcations, etc. Instead of choosing one
arbitrary area, it was decided to look into how FOSS are currently solving these problems related
to project delays. Thus, the current situation can be addressed and utilised whilst ﬁnding a
root-cause for problems. It was apparent that 5 initiatives were started (Described in Section
4.2) which needed to be explained by stakeholders themselves. The initiatives aimed at reducing
project delays and improving the quality of projects by creating tools and techniques that can
be used across all departments at FOSS.
What was apparent from speaking to multiple stakeholders, was the lack of risk management
being implemented during the innovative initiatives.
This meant that schedule overruns in
projects were common, as a lack of major risks were prominent. The risks identiﬁed by the
researcher were mainly: strategic risks, customer risks and competitive risks (Deﬁned in more
detail on Section 3.2). Hence, the 5 initiatives were looking into maintaining innovation but
not through risk management techniques. To connect the risk management and maintaining
innovation, it is important to describe the 5 initiatives and scope the project to examine 1
initiative only.
4.2
Five Initiatives Started at FOSS to Reduce Project Delays
In order to improve the R&D process within FOSS, ﬁve initiatives were developed with 2 year
plans in mind. They are set to ﬁnish and be implemented by the ﬁnal quarter of 2020. As
described in the literature review (Section 3.2), initiatives can be hazardous in regard to an
increase of cost and the risk of a hindrance outweighing beneﬁts. For each initiative, a diagnoses
plan, principle plan and ambition was created. Figure 18 represents the overall names given to
each initiative. From there, a short description can be given. This Thesis will describe each
individual initiative then relate this back into FOSS.
30
==PAGE:41==
Figure 18: The Five Initiatives Started by FOSS
Figure 18 shows the original start and end date of the 5 initiatives. Alongside this, it gives a
small overview of the title and how it will go about solving a speciﬁc issue in FOSS. From here,
all 5 initiatives are described in a greater detail. More speciﬁcally, why FOSS needed to start
the initiative.
Initiative 1 - Demand Driven Pipeline Solution Pipeline and Roadmap
Initiative 1 was created in order for FOSS to become more demand driven in regard to respon-
siveness to customers. Before this initiative began, FOSS was purely technology driven. Due to
the VUCA environment of the system, a roadmap established to outline how to become demand
driven. There are two sides to this initiative, the ﬁrst being the commercial side coupled to the
roadmap. By using front end theory, stakeholders meet once a week to discuss the process of
projects. The second is a platform for projects which will be added to the initial roadmap in a
latter phase.
Initiative 2 - Aligned Requirements, Speciﬁcations and Validation
This initiative considered which requirements the management are responsible for. Prior to this,
many employees were confused which stakeholder was responsible for separate tasks within mile-
stones. This was especially present in development projects as the the management recognised
one of the weak points for FOSS was requirement management.
31
==PAGE:42==
Initiative 3 - Integrating Voice-of-Customer into Daily Work
Although the Voice of Customer (VoC) was taken advantage of prior to projects kicking oﬀ, FOSS
failed to react during and concurrent to starting projects. By using the VoC at a pragmatic level,
it was decided to create a design board. This allowed an interaction across all the development
departments and justiﬁed the VoC to each one.
Initiative 4 - Leadership and Employee/Talent Development
A decision was taken to implement a table group.
This table was implemented in order to
increase organisational health. Before the initiation started - FOSS’ project management was
almost non-existent. Therefore, a managerial decision was made to develop the employees to
receive a better understanding of how to improve their time awareness for innovation.
Initiative 5 - Improving Organisational Performance and Capabilities
Two key aspects were inﬂuential within the ﬁfth initiative. Firstly, FOSS aimed at focusing on a
project management package. The second was known as Steam Clarity. This, if Stream Clarity
is correctly implemented, it will also aid in the project management package. This initiative
aims at building on and improving what FOSS have instead of starting from scratch. The basic
structure is acceptable at ”Level 1” but lacking much details at ”Level 2” (These levels are
explained in more detail in Section 4.3). Alongside this, poor implementation has been noticed
by management. This initiative had a number of advantages if correctly implemented such as:
improve reviews at stream level; balance out the PMs responsibility across all streams; build
a stable base for continuous improvements for future projects; easier and improved training of
project team members.
Which Initiative was Chosen and Why
Initiative 5 was speciﬁcally chosen to endeavour into during this report in order to scope down
and ﬁnd a root-cause for project delays. This was because the main contact in FOSS advised
to contribute towards this initiative because more transparent data was available to eventuate
an analysis. Before diving into the initiative, a clear boundary needs to be set in regards to
the stakeholders.
Therefore, it is essential to begin with an area of eﬀect in regards to the
departments and stakeholders.
From the chosen initiative, the 12 departments at FOSS (known as streams) will be described
and how this is related to the Stage Gate Model.
32
==PAGE:43==
4.3
12 Departments at FOSS and How They Interlink to the Stage Gate
Model
To better understand the working conditions within the R&D sector of FOSS, this section
was created. FOSS’s departments use work-streams (streams) to progressively complete large
projects by having multiple groups work simultaneously.
All streams are described with a
detailed description of how it is related to initiative 5. Initiative 5 revolved around Stream
Clarity (Described in Section 4.2). Stream Clarity looked into the 12 streams currently within the
R&D segment and ﬁnding a communication platform template to better document data. Thus,
enabling a transparent and simple transfer of known knowledge from one stream to another, and
reducing project delays. The 12 streams at FOSS are described below:
1. Customer Value - Department of the business development. Known for identifying and
qualifying opportunities into projects. Extensive research prior and during projects to
create value for the customer.
2. FOSS Value - The core value that the company follows in regard to all projects. These
inspire the other streams to abide and constrain to certain advantageous actions.
3. Hardware (HW) - Part of the R&D sector. Contains two areas known as Mechanics and
Electronic. These are essential for developing prototypes in the development streams.
4. Software (SW) - Part of the R&D sector. Works alongside the HW stream to develop
prototypes.
5. Digital Business (DigBiz) - This area is outside the other streams and works digi-
tally to enhance FOSS systems. Products can become more intelligent through upfront
requirements.
6. Application - Highly sophisticated stream which measures chain value. Deep knowledge
analysis on raw material data. Central to the R&D sector.
7. User Experience (UX) - Develops a concept design report which stipulates all the user
need requirements. The UX also looks into state-of-the-art solutions available as well as
continuous customer requirements.
8. Reliability, Environmental, Legal (REL) - Also known as Veriﬁcation and Validation
(VV). Using the UX stream, requirements are set which are used as a validation on when
to start and ﬁnish modules or systems.
9. Sales and Marketing - Field test and market trials of products. This happens after
Gate 6 (G6). This is the ﬁrst time the product is released to the real-world.
10. Service - The product is installed at a customer and the stream overtakes the overall
responsibility of the instrument. This is the ﬁrst time it is released from the R&D sector
and into the testing and launch phase.
11. Supply Chain - Also known as the production segment. They are responsible for pro-
ducing a set amount of products for the market.
12. Project Management (PM) - Responsible for overlooking all streams. Ensuring that
all streams are correctly operating and critical paths are reached.
33
==PAGE:44==
FOSS currently use the Coopers Stage Gate Model (as described in Section 2.2.2) to have an
overview of their projects. To see how the streams are related to initiative 5 and Coopers Stage
Gate Model, ﬁgure 19 was created:
Figure 19: An Integration of Cooper’s Stage Gate Model and FOSS’s 12 Streams
The ﬁrst diﬀerence between Cooper’s Stage Gate model and how it is used by FOSS, is that
instead of having 5 gates, there are 7. It still follows the same basic principle but is adapted to
their innovative mindset on projects. The ﬁrst process step FOSS follows is Identify Opportunity.
After gate 1 is passed, the Design Concept and Prepare Development Authorisation can start.
Thereafter, Deﬁning the Project Architecture and Finalise Design of Total Solution. This leads
to the Mature Technical Solution and a Veriﬁcation of the Total Solution. The ﬁnal two process
after gate 5 is the Ramp up for Sales Release and Launch and Transfer to Line Organisation.
Below the 7 gates, the 12 streams are mentioned. All streams have diﬀerent stakeholders which
are mentioned within the Appendix Section 9.2. It is important to note that this integration
was used for a number of years with no documentation. Therefore, stakeholders have adjusted
to this approach, making it more challenging to introduce Process Templates which will aid on
time constraints to projects.
Before initialising initiative 5, the streams documentation was limited and streams rarely
collaborated as mentioned. This is because there are diﬀerent departments in separate segments
of the organisation.
Figure 20 shows a blank version of how all streams documented their
completion of events. This is known as a Level 1 Process Template documentation for FOSS.
An example of a ﬁlled in template has been created for this Thesis on ﬁgure 47 (Appendix
Section 9.3). It shows how complicated a small project can become in regards to communication
34
==PAGE:45==
with all streams.
Figure 20: Level 1 Process Blank Example
The Level 1 Process consists of all streams on the vertical axis of ﬁgure 20. The horizontal axis
has the gates that FOSS follow (gate 2 and 4 are split into a and b as these are larger gates). The
entire project was documented using only this template. This meant that a lack of detail was
often seen. As explained in the literature (Section 3.3), a lack of documentation can lead to a
ﬁne line between balancing the innovation of organisation and adapting to unforeseen situations.
On the other hand, an increased level of documentation can speed up similar projects and aid
crucial decisions for FOSS. Furthermore, each stream does not have to communicate with the
other streams necessarily, so there is a absence of amity. These two risks have according to
some stakeholders, led to a delayed project in regard to time and an increased cost for a project.
This lack of transparency between streams could also lead to a long run loss of a competitive
advantage in the market (As explained in Section 3.1). To overcome these issues within initiative
5, FOSS needed to rework the original Level 1 Process Template. But they wanted to base it
on the same streams and the same gates whilst essentially increasing the documentation level.
Two key aspects to aid with the streams were added. First were the ”Leads” of the streams
- known to have the project view. This stakeholder is responsible for keeping time and budget
constraints.
The second was the ”Stream Owner” - known to have the process view.
This
stakeholder is responsible for the documentation of each step of the project. This does not mean
each day the Stream Owner needs to be present but rather that the documentation is correctly
ﬁlled out by the project team.
35
==PAGE:46==
To show how this eﬀects the original ﬁgure 19, another similar ﬁgure 21 was created. This
now has the two added stakeholder responsibilities.
Figure 21: Integrating the Stream Owners and Project Leads into the Current Streams
It is important to note that the 12 streams have remained the same along with the original 7
gates which have all been described within the Analysis of Interviews and Workshop (Section
4). Furthermore, the stakeholder responsible and in charge of the ”Individual Projects” is called
the Lead of the stream. It is not called this within the ﬁgure because there can be multiple leads
of streams depending on the project at hand.
Before going into depth about the Level 2 Process Template, one must remember that the
majority of employees within FOSS are from technical backgrounds. This, as some literature
suggests, may dictate that documentation level is missing because of their innovative problem
solving. Literature looking into this theory is mentioned within the Innovative Literature Re-
view (Section 3.3). Therefore implementing a complicated template is precarious and unwanted
by FOSS at this time.
That being mentioned, the Level 2 Process Template created for initiative 5 and its analysis can
be seen within the following section.
4.4
Level 2 Process Template Analysis
The Level 2 Process Template consists of three methodologies into one ﬁgure. It was created
in initiative 5 to reduce project delays and improve documentation levels within projects. To
36
==PAGE:47==
understand the problem and solution space, all methodologies are explained within Section 2. It
is important to note that FOSS have adapted these methodologies to better ﬁt their organisation.
This section will show how or if they are adapted then represent the advantages and limitations
to the three methodologies chosen. This is an essential foothold to analyse the Interviews and
Workshop within the next section.
Figure 22: Level 2 Process Blank Example
To analyse how the limitations and beneﬁts of each methodology used, the following three
subsections were created.
4.4.1
Coopers Stage Gate Model Analysis
The ﬁrst methodology relies on Coopers Stage Gate Model (Methodology Described on Section
2.2.2) much like Level 1. This sets the gate descriptions and allows the project to run in a
Waterfall methodological way. There are numerous advantages for FOSS using this methodology
this way:
−A simple roadmap for stakeholders
−Shows steps at a project team level
−Quality of execution is increased due to less critical errors in the process
−Cross-functional inputs from all stakeholders
−Increased project timeline from less intermediate errors
The ﬁrst advantage is that it is a simple roadmap style approach which all stakeholders involved
can follow. This means that any new employees or stakeholders can follow the process map and
37
==PAGE:48==
be reassured that the previous gate has been successfully passed. Secondly, all steps from the
start of the project to the end can be tracked and known at a project level. This ensures that
more documentation can be kept for future projects. Thus the more completed projects, the
shorter time span and less risk will be occurred. Thirdly, because of the step-by-step guideline,
critical errors should be reduced or at least mitigated. Which in turn, increased the quality of
project execution. Fourthly, the streams will collaborate circumscribed, which allows for more
inputs from diversely appropriate project teams. Finally, due to the aforementioned decrease in
errors, the timeline of projects should be dramatically decreased.
Therefore, the adapted Coopers Stage Gate Model that is to be implemented in FOSS, is ex-
tremely important to ensure that steps are not overlooked.
But this can lead to potential
limitations:
−Due to the sequential activities, it leaves no room for agility
−Strain can become present between the organisation and creative thinking
−Utilising customers to its full potential rarely works
Once a gate is closed, according to Cooper’s theory (Explained in the Literature Review Section
3.4), you should not go back and amend (much like the Waterfall methodology). This can lead
to problems later in the project if complications arise because there is no agility present. As de-
scribed in the literature review, agility is one of the ways to bridge the gap between an innovative
organisation and reducing project delays. Secondly, the model because of its step-by-step guide,
can lead to less creativity within the organisation. Leaving innovative thinkers with diﬃculties
because of the resistance of the Waterfall type methodology. Finally, although customers need
to be asked and showed the product at each closure of the gate (according to Cooper’s theory),
this is extremely challenging. And even if performed, the opinion of customers within products
usually does not yield insights into disruptive innovations. Therefore, the utilisation is often
dismissed and not used within Coopers Stage Gate Model.
This adapted Cooper’s Stage Gate model is mainly used for maintaining and ensuring documen-
tation is upheld.
4.4.2
RASCI Analysis
The second Methodology is RASCI (Section 2.2.3) which is a responsibility matrix. Allowing
all team members to recognise and know which stakeholder is accountable for a speciﬁc step is
crucial for holding liability. Again, there are advantages and limitations to using the RASCI
methodology. The advantages are:
−Setting clear expectations for stakeholders
−Avoiding work and stakeholder overloads
−Streamlining communication within projects
The ﬁrst advantage to using RASCI is that towards the beginning of project, boundaries are
set for all stakeholders. These boundaries allow the ability to clearly see who is responsible
38
==PAGE:49==
for which aspect. Thus eliminating confusion by knowing who is accountable for the task com-
pletion. The second advantage is because of the same reasons as the ﬁrst advantage, it allows
all stakeholders to see the amount of work expectation. Due to this reason, it should allow a
mitigation of having one single point of failure, where knowledge and responsibility rests on one
stakeholder, therefore creating silos. Alongside this, instead of having all stakeholders opinions
on each task, RASCI allows a distinction between consulted and informed. Leaving only the
Incorporated ideas of stakeholders who are needed for that task. The ﬁnal advantage and the
most important for FOSS, is the streamline of communication. Involving the correct people at
the right time, speeding up projects whilst improving decision making, allows a streamline of
correct communication. The RASCI methodology also has limitations:
−No clariﬁcation of actions within a project
−Multiple stakeholders responsibilities are diﬃcult
There are two major limitations for using the RASCI methodology. The ﬁrst is no clariﬁcation
of actions within the project. This can lead to confused stakeholders if the Level 2 Process
Template is not suﬃciently ﬁlled in. The second is that assigning one stakeholder responsible
for a large task is troublesome. Therefore, many assign multiple stakeholders to ensure the task
is completed faster. But this juxtaposes the RASCI methodology of assigning responsibility to
one stakeholder. Hence, the project owner needs to ensure it is correctly used.
The RASCI methodology is mainly used for appointing stakeholders responsibilities.
4.4.3
SIPOC Analysis
The third methodology is SIPOC (Section 2.2.4) which identiﬁes 5 key areas of the process map.
A complicated environment can be characterised in regards to the Supplier, Input, Process,
Output and the Customer. This logical framework aids the project team to develop an input
into an output. There are three advantages for FOSS in regards to the SIPOC methodology:
−Setting process boundaries with each task
−Important level of details for the project team
−Considering the end-to-end detailed process
In addition to setting the boundaries in regards to the stakeholders, one must set task boundaries
as well. The SIPOC allows an overview of these boundaries with the input and output deﬁantly
stated. This leads onto the second advantage which is a valuable level of details for the project
team themselves. Due to the complexity described in the Streams segment (Section 4.3). The
details allow the project team to understand the process revolving around the supplier and the
customer. The ﬁnal advantage to the SIPOC methodology, is the entire process is an end-to-end
development. Once all tasks are aligned and the project team understands for whom they are
delivering too, a transparent level of data can be reached. Thus allowing for a comprehensive
analysis without neglecting of critical stakeholders.
Once again, this methodology does have some drawbacks. These are mentioned below:
39
==PAGE:50==
−Requires a certain level of detail
−Incorrect outputs from project team
Much like the previous methodologies, the SIPOC needs a level of detail which allows the next
stream and customers to understand the thinking process of the project team. If this is neglected
then the SIPOC becomes a burdensome. The second limitation is the incorrect outputs placed
into SIPOC. Within this methodology, it is essential that the project team understands which
stream will take over from their completed task. If this is misunderstood or incorrectly inputted
then this could create unnecessary time delays. Alongside this, an incorrect input could lead to
undesired outputs.
4.4.4
Collective Level 2 Template Analysis
The three methodologies are combined into one template known as the Level 2 Template. By
having these into 1 template, the disadvantages are limited which allows FOSS to have a com-
prehensive tool. The tool was created in order to reduce the project delays and increase docu-
mentation. Although the surface looks simple, the interaction and communication between each
stream becomes excessively complicated within a few sprints.
The advantages of combining
these are mentioned below:
−Stakeholders responsibilities are described in detail
−Simple template to follow for stakeholders
−Potential scope and quality of execution increased
The ﬁrst advantage is that stakeholders are now fully aware of their responsibilities each task.
This allows for a clear understanding to the project team who should be contacted. The second
advantage is that the template is simple and clear to follow for stakeholders. Allowing new
stakeholders to grasp the importance of the Level 2 Template.
The ﬁnal advantage is the
potential to increase the timeline of a project by improving the scope and quality of the project.
This does lead to some overall limitations of the Level 2 Process:
−No timeline mentioned for dates
−Quantitative methodology is missing
−Potential lack of documentation on each individual task
The ﬁrst limitation is that there is no timeline mentioned for the stakeholders.
This could
potentially lead to streams not understanding when they should begin their part within the
project. A second limitation is that there is no quantitative methodology used. This can lead
to a diﬀerentiation in interpretation of the Level 2 Template. The ﬁnal limitation is a lack of
documentation for each task. Because stakeholders may have not documented anything before,
they may disregard this template as well if they do not deem it helpful.
The Level 2 Template Analysis is followed by an analysis of selected interviews and a workshop.
40
==PAGE:51==
5
Analysis of the Interviews and Workshop at FOSS
Through the analysis of FOSS, the problem within initiative 5 has been described. Alongside
this, what FOSS has put in place has been analysed in regard to the Level 2 Process Template.
The analysis will use this and a further investigation through interviews and a workshop to ﬁnd
root-cause(s) of project delays. How and under which condition will be explained further on.
Thus, adding to the Level 2 Process Template to mitigate further project delays. It begins with
an analysis of multiple interviews conducted by FOSS stakeholders. Thereafter, triangulation of
the interviews with a workshop will be conducted.
5.1
7 Interviews Conducted with FOSS Stakeholders
5.1.1
Deﬁning the Context of the Interviews
The interviews were conducted at FOSS prior to the COVID-19 outbreak of 2020. This is impor-
tant to note as they could not be conducted in parallel because stakeholders were unavailable and
less willing to have second interviews since most questions were answered within this interview
session. The interviews were therefore organised and regulated by two Post doc students also
working alongside FOSS in risk management (the co-supervisors of this Masters Thesis). All
stakeholders were asked to clarify their position within the company, how long they had worked
at FOSS and their take on problems within the R&D process. No stakeholders will be named in
order to keep anonymity. Deductive approaches were incorporated into the interviews to ensure
that a structured and predetermined framework could be applied to each stakeholder. This
allows for a relatively quick reply and genuine answers that are not biased by secondary eﬀects.
The limitations of using deductive is that it is less ﬂexible and in depth and theory developed
answers are less common. Due to the time constraints of all researches involved, the inductive
approach was avoided. Thereafter, a thematic approach will be used to identify patterns and
provide a rich interpretation of the overall study. This approach uses Codes to decipher the
frequency of responses and the sequence of causation by diﬀerent stakeholders. This will allow
a causal chain of events to be found and a root-cause analysis can be conducted.
The interviews as mentioned previously were conducted by two Post doc students who are
looking into Risk Management.
Both were present at all interviews and all interviews were
recorded. The level of English diﬀered from one another; this made interpreting and under-
standing Danish essential for some stakeholders.
This is crucial to understand as diﬀerent
cultures explain and construe questions in distinctive manners. However, the cultural aspect
will not be considered as formerly mentioned. Furthermore, because it was only recorded and
not on video, the non-verbal communication is also disregarded (See ﬁgure for 13 the deﬁnition).
All interviews have a transcript in Appendix Section 9.4. Although multiple stakeholders from
various departments were interviewed all across FOSS, only those who are within the System
Demarcation were included to limit the scope of the analysis.
41
==PAGE:52==
5.1.2
Gathering the Information on the Interviews
Out of 31 interviews conducted, only 7 were selected in this analysis. This was because of the
scope of the analysis (R&D only). The 7 interviews were all from various R&D managerial
departments across FOSS. All interviews varied in length from 45minutes to 2hours. The tran-
script of each interview was then created (See Appendix 9.4) in order to understand the text as
well as the discussion. The interviews were analysed both by questions which were set by the
Post doc students and the literature review (Section 3.2.2).
Thereafter, Atlas.ti was used to analyse the qualitative data. Atlas.ti is a simple software which
counts the number of times throughout an interview, a stakeholder mentions a topic. It is used
throughout the interview process to scope which root-cause should be analysed to reduce project
delays at FOSS. All ﬁles on Atlas.ti can be found on Appendix Section 9.5.
The interviews were split into 2 speciﬁc sections. The ﬁrst is the technical terminology; (this
answers questions such as: What goes wrong within the process? Why it goes wrong? What
can be done to ﬁx the project delays? How do you think it can be ﬁxed? etc). The second part
is where in the stage gate model is the most problematic in terms of a speciﬁc gate.
The idea of the interviews is two-fold; the ﬁrst is to ﬁnd the most problematic project delays
and the second is to ﬁnd exactly where these occur. This will allow for a root-cause analysis to
pinpoint and scope down to ﬁxing a speciﬁc unsatisfactory area.
There were 11 codes which were found by Atlas.ti to be essential and repetitive over more than
1 stakeholder. Each of these codes need an explanation and deﬁnition to understand:
1. High Cost - Where a stakeholder mentions that the monetary cost is immense, especially
compared to the initial cost of the project.
2. Marketing - Any words regarding the marketing and selling of the products.
3. Utilisation of Resources - Regarding the implementation and application of employees
and material possessions around FOSS.
4. Project Management - Where leadership is lacking or a neglecting the correct manner-
ism for the project team.
5. Quality - Where the quality of the end product is questioned somewhere in the Stage
Gate Model.
6. Speciﬁcations - Production and creation of physical products in regard to an original
drawing or speciﬁc requests from customers.
7. Testing - The development of prototypes to evaluate the design of the end product.
8. Time Restraints - Any vocabulary or conversational codes that mention the time projects
(normally extended periods of extra time).
9. Veriﬁcation - Establishing and understanding if the stakeholders trust other departments’
accuracy.
10. Customers (VoC) - When the customer and how their requests were handled throughout
the project.
42
==PAGE:53==
11. Documentation - Stakeholders describing evidence of recording the projects or lack there
of within all departments.
These codes were then analysed within the 7 interviews on Atlas.ti to ﬁnd patterns and phrases
that matched the above mentioned 11 codes. All the ﬁles and interviews put into Atlas.ti can
be found in the Appendix Section 9.5. The results were then put into a bar-chart to see the end
results in ﬁgure 23.
Figure 23: Bar-chart representing the Atlas.ti terminology codes generated
As seen in ﬁgure 23, there are ﬁve terms that need to be analysed: Project Management, Quality,
Speciﬁcations, Time Restraints and Veriﬁcation. Five were chosen due to time constraints of
the Thesis (Described in more detail on Section 7.3). Before the ﬁve topics can be analysed
further, the second part of the Atlas.ti analysis needs to be discussed. The second part was to
ﬁnd where these problems occur most frequently. Because FOSS uses the stage gate model, it
was split into the separate gates and explained below:
1. All Gates - This is deﬁned as the stakeholder referring to the entire Stage Gate Model
as a whole.
2. Gate 0 - Prior to the Stage Gate Model starting, all information gathered from all de-
partments happens in the ”Identify Opportunity” stage.
43
==PAGE:54==
3. Gate 1 - To pass gate 1, the opportunity is identiﬁed and the ”Design Concept” can begin.
4. Gate 2 - After designing the concept, gate 2 is passed and ”Deﬁning the Architecture”
can start.
5. Gate 3 - The ﬁnalisation of the design in regards to the total solution can begin.
6. Gate 4 - This consists of passing through both gate 4 and 4b where the veriﬁcation of the
solution and maturing of technical solution between multiple departments is initiated.
7. Gate 5 - After the solution is completely veriﬁed, the ”Ramp up for Sales Release” is
begun by the production team.
8. Gate 6 - This is where the product begins its ”Launch” phase into the market.
9. Gate 7 - After the product has ﬁnished its launch and is known in the market.
Figure 24: Bar-chart representing the Atlas.ti stage gate model codes generated
It is clear to see that Gate 2 has the largest proportion according to the interviews.
From
these results, according to the How to Analyse Qualitative Data (Explained in Section 2.3.3),
an analysis of the various elements of the material is performed. It will include both parts of
the interview analysis.
It is important to note at this point that the remaining 24 interviews that were not selected for
this analysis have the same pattern in Atlas.ti as the 7 interviews but these again, were not in
scope. Henceforth, if a larger analysis was to be created with the same data, the same outcome
will occur.
44
==PAGE:55==
5.1.3
Analysing the Content of the Interviews
From the results found in the ﬁgure 23 and 24, it is clear that ﬁve categories stand out: Project
Management, Quality, Speciﬁcations, Time Restraints and Veriﬁcation and one speciﬁc gate will
be analysed: Gate 2. Only ﬁve were chosen because of time constraints within the project. For
future research, all the 11 items should be looked into with all gates to be more eﬃcient. In order
to understand how this was perceived by diﬀerent stakeholders and assimilating a root-cause
analysis, one must assemble quotes into a coherent constituent. Hence, the ﬁve categories and
gate 2 with their main arguments and propositions need to be aligned.
Project Management
According to multiple of the stakeholders, the strategic approach taken by project managers has
dramatically changed over the course of the previous years. This is because FOSS are aiming
to have a better overview of the full solution, before committing resources. This has however
lead to numerous accusations that documentation and vital features have not been upheld by
management. This has lead to the risk that requirements and capabilities of the product and
end users have gone amiss.
One of the project management responsibilities is also to ensure that products are to a high
quality standard. This however cannot be simply checked at the end of a project, but intermit-
tently and between gates. As of now, some of the stakeholders see the approach as bottom up,
instead of a top down approach. This has apparently lead to misleading information regarding
the strategic and business approach of certain projects.
Quality
This is a very ambiguous term which, during the interviews, was asked to be deﬁned and
described by each individual stakeholder. To see each of the deﬁnitions, the transcripts (Section
9.4) can be read.
According to 4 stakeholders, many prototypes lack quality due to the original supply of ma-
terials.
This occasionally causes delays on projects and customers can often be unsatisﬁed.
Unfortunately, one of the main contributors to this quality is the lack of testing and maintaining
a healthy customer relationship throughout a project (not just the start and the end).
Moreover, the stakeholders mention that there is little focus on quality measurements because
of limited quality management at FOSS. This according to one of the stakeholders is because of
functional inhibition collaboration. Meaning that, various departments in the company are not
collaborating eﬃciently of eﬀectively enough, so the end-goals are not aligned.
Speciﬁcations
Features are often missed because FOSS currently uses only Microsoft Excel and do not track
changes by stakeholders. Therefore, features and speciﬁcations can be deleted if they are deemed
45
==PAGE:56==
too challenging and henceforth, the customer does not receive their original requirements. Es-
pecially between departments, speciﬁcations seem to be lost according to some stakeholders.
Moreover, the inspections on individual parts are less examined the longer the project takes.
This is often due to the speciﬁcations becoming more lenient as stakeholders do not want an
even more delayed project. This is normally due to the nondisciplinary aspect of FOSS. As
multiple stakeholders aforementioned: ”FOSS is like a start-up company around processes.”
In regard to the speciﬁcations the process is currently a checklist. This is not updated nor
reviewed many times during the project as of now. As one stakeholder commented: ”It is a
bit all over the place right now.” More involvement from departments and better deﬁnitions
would sharpen speciﬁcations throughout the projects. A project which is running alongside this
Thesis is known as Dexter. It is being used as a baseline or how to write speciﬁcations and
follow them throughout the project. This project was diﬀerent from other similar projects, as a
speciﬁc emphasis was placed on project managers to set speciﬁcations and keep them in mind
during all meetings, much as the literature suggests (See Section 3.1).
Time Restraints
Six of out seven of the stakeholders mentioned that time was one of the largest issues in projects.
A deadline is set for projects at but the four projects looked into during this Thesis, all ran
over the initial deadline. Depending on which stakeholder was spoken too, there were numerous
reasons for this. The ﬁrst being the development team taking longer than planned. Being part
of the R&D sector, this has lead to a loss of trust by sales and marketing. Henceforth, the
timeline is reputable and not believed.
Another aspect is that modules used in prototype development are old. These need to be re-
engineered but because of time restraints, this is diﬃcult to complete. This apparently leads to
a lower quality of end products and a postponement of new technology being created.
Within the departments, it is jokingly said to take 7 years to learn everything about the company.
This, although commented in a facetiously respect, represents the diﬃculties in learning the
entire manufacturing and development process. Henceforth, new employees take a longer and
protracted length of time to productively add proﬁciency to processes.
Veriﬁcation
As of now, high risk parts within the development and creation of products are not known.
Therefore, veriﬁcation of the end product is diﬃcult to determine, not knowing how many high
risk parts are within a device. Also, once a high risk part is eventually identiﬁed and the suppliers
are aware, certain questions are asked. Such as: ”What is wrong with the part?”, ”How can
you tell that the part could become faulty?”, ”What checks have been completed to show it is
high risk?”. These cannot be answered by FOSS as there are no veriﬁcation instruments as of
now. One stakeholder advised that resources should be better divided in order to be prepared
for veriﬁcation tactics.
46
==PAGE:57==
Another element to consider, is the idea that requirements and veriﬁcation need to be tested
throughout a project. The New Product Development (NPD) department should be responsible
for this but they do not set aside additional time to deal with testing. This should be the norm
apparently but time and resources for veriﬁcation is not utilised eﬃciently.
As mentioned within the Speciﬁcations above, the Dexter project was a systematic project that
followed strict speciﬁcations and veriﬁcations. The project team has little parking time and
have built a new way of verifying UX is satisﬁed. This will be looked into during the root-cause
analysis.
It was also important to analyse the gate where these problems occur. This, according to ﬁgure
24 was gate 2. The only gate that will be looked into during the interview analysis is gate 2
because of how out of proportion it seems.
Gate 2
Out of the 7 gates within the Stage Gate Model, passing through gate 2 seemed to be mentioned
and questioned the most. Gate 2 is between ”Designing the Concept” and ”Deﬁning the Project
Architecture”. This according to one stakeholder is due to the sheer amount of projects that are
on hold at Gate 2 or will be stopped here. At this point, the risk of moving onto a project is still
extremely high and many uncertainties (unknown unknowns) are present. Therefore, rigorous
research is conducted before passing through this gate, sometimes for years. At this gate, the
project team is also expected to review and estimate time delays within the project. This is
apparently always underestimated and the critical path seems to be missed with many major
projects.
Furthermore, features, which are currently only placed on Microsoft Excel are neglected or
removed because they are an inconvenience for the project team. These features are however
requested by customers and some are fundamental in making sure the product reaches a large
market. These features are only realised to be missing towards the end of a project when a
review ream looks into the beginning requirements and speciﬁcations. Thus, being too late to
stabilise and secure any errors.
On the other hand, Gate 2 has a measurement chain evaluation which is well understood and
documented by one department.
47
==PAGE:58==
5.1.4
Review the Results of the Interviews
The qualitative interviews were essential to base an initial root-cause analysis on and be able to
conduct the workshop. It showed that many areas across all departments need to be improved,
especially within gate 2. The project management seems to change depending on the project
with speciﬁcations and veriﬁcations missing often.
Time restraints also seems to be a key
aspect of delays amongst projects with poor planning being one of the main causes for concern.
Interviews however have limitations, hence why a root-cause analysis cannot simple be based on
them. A limitation of interviews was therefore created.
5.1.5
Limitations of the Interviews
The in-depth interviews conducted were essential for the ﬁrst stage of the analysis of the inter-
views and workshop (Section 5) but there are multiple limitations that were upheld throughout.
Firstly, the researcher was not speciﬁcally involved in creating a risk management interview.
Henceforth, the scope was large and did not contain the exact outline that was needed for a
clear, structured analysis. Secondly, depending on the time, day and workload of the stakehold-
ers, answers can vary dramatically. For example, if a meeting was held prior to the interviews,
information would be relayed and bias towards the main topics of the meeting. This would there-
fore eﬀect the overall codes that were used in the Atlas.ti patterns. Thirdly, the understanding
of questions and interpretation can greatly reduce the accuracy of answers. This meaning that
the stakeholders rely heavily on perceiving ﬂuent English questions. If not, then perceptions
can tremendously ﬂuctuate. Finally, a lack of accessibility to information can put pressure and
stress on a stakeholder.
Unlike emails, where information is readily available and time can
be spent on answering questions thoroughly, an interview can invoke undesirable answers that
are misunderstood and juxtapose numerous other stakeholders. Because of these limitations,
triangulation was used and the interview analysis was combined with a workshop analysis.
5.2
Workshop Conducted with FOSS Stakeholders
In accordance to the triangulation theory (Section 2.3.1), at least two sources need to be taken
into consideration to ﬁnd a root-cause. A workshop was conducted which included the same
stakeholders as the interviews in the previous section. The workshop allows for not only an
individual reﬂection upon problems at FOSS, but also a group discussion where issues can be
raised and questioned. Henceforth, opinions can be changed and disputed instantaneously.
5.2.1
Deﬁning the Context of the Workshop
The workshop was a one day event conducted at FOSS. All stakeholders were introduced one-by-
one to ensure that the departments were clear and their current projects discussed. The set-up
allowed all stakeholders to see each other and they could choose their own seating plan. Once
again, due to time constraints, a deductive approach was used, which was explained during
the interview analysis. The workshop was however not recorded. Again, an analysis of the
48
==PAGE:59==
interviews and workshop (Section 5) will be used to decipher the end results and create a root-
cause analysis. A standard workshop approach was followed which consisted of 5 major steps,
explained within Gathering the Information below.
5.2.2
Gathering the Information of the Workshop
The workshop was broken down into several steps to make it interesting and to attain the most
useful information for this Thesis. The steps are listed in chronological order:
1. Introduction
The workshop began with an introduction on why they were selected and the idea of the
entire workshop. This included a brief PowerPoint and a handout of blank sheets of paper
alongside the printed papers needed for later
2. Menti Exercise
A code was then used for all the stakeholders to join. A list of all possible risks were asked
and ﬁgure 25 below represents this:
Figure 25: Menti Word Diagram from the Stakeholders at the Workshop
This step allowed all stakeholders to have a visual representation of everyone’s point of
view. No-one was singled out to explain what they meant but it set the scope for the
following step.
3. Individual Brainstorm
A blank piece of paper was handed to each stakeholder which can be seen on ﬁgure 26.
The categories were chosen based on grouped streams and are shown on the left hand side
49
==PAGE:60==
of the ﬁgure. As mentioned previously, diﬀerent technical terminology is used compared
to the interviews because these terms are better known to FOSS stakeholders. Therefore,
it made the exercise more concise and easier to follow for them. Towards the bottom are
the gates that need to be passed. Because some of the risks run over more than one gate,
it was decided not to create vertical lines down towards the gate but rather leave it open
to interpretation.
Figure 26: Blank Sheet Handed to each Stakeholder at the Workshop

could cause an uncertainty or risk within that particular gate.
5. Feedback
At the end of the workshop, a feedback session commenced.
This allowed any extra
information that the stakeholders thought was overlooked, to be discussed.
50
==PAGE:61==
5.2.3
Analysing the Content of the Workshop
In order to analyse the workshops, the joint brainstorm was the culminating eﬀect, henceforth,
this will be scrutinised. It will be split into the same categories as the interviews to compare
the results 1 to 1. This could only be completed by adopting the language FOSS uses then
modifying this into the categories detected by Atlas.ti in the interviews. This also allows a
greater triangulation during the root-cause analysis stage. It is important to note within the
workshop analysis that the gates are not split up to be analysed (like in the interviews) because
there are clear characteristics of each category within the workshop. All the results received in
the workshop were placed into Appendix Section 9.6.
Project Management
Within the project management, the main risk was the lack of understanding of the problem,
which can create huge variations in the quality of customer demands. In turn, this can lead
to massive knock on eﬀects throughout the process if the customer is not involved further.
Furthermore, towards G2, project processes are not the main focus until deep into the develop-
ment. This can lead to a less attractive oﬀering in regard to the top-down approach of some
projects after G2. Customers and UX have diﬀerent prototypes to those created in house. This,
along with the aforementioned problems lead to an accumulation of unsolved problems which
are too late and expensive to solve. Throughout the project, often minor errors accumulate
and cause a knock-on eﬀect of delays. Finally, poor planning throughout all gates, can lead to
wrong priorities within a project. This causes time delays, cost overruns and incorrect resource
management.
Quality
Between G1 and G2, reference software is often not fully understood nor utilised. This adds a
layer of extra complexity to a project and leads to a lack of quality on projects. Robust design
principles are not applied which can lead to a loss of quality within G3. Instrument solutions
are often missing in quality requirement validation which can freeze designs.
Furthermore,
suppliers need to know quality expectations prior to the creation of prototypes or a confusion
in management leads to a lack of conﬁguration management throughout the project.
Speciﬁcations
Prior to initiating a project, work instructions can be unclear which can in turn be a speciﬁcation
risk further in the project. Furthermore, strategies speciﬁcally for ensuring all speciﬁcations are
met, are lacking from G2 - G4. A lack of documentation and predictability can occur towards
G6, leading to customer speciﬁcations going amiss.
This generally leads to non-conforming
Standard Operating Procedures (SOP) and non-updated assembly instructions.
Henceforth,
speciﬁcations set by FOSS and by customers are unclear throughout the stage gate model.
51
==PAGE:62==
Time Restraints
Suppliers are often chosen based on previous projects and not on the best ﬁt for the project. This
can mean that suppliers are outside the strategic ﬁt of the project. In turn, suppliers often send
components late, which leads to a time restraint for the manufacturing department. Critical
components can also be missed at G0 if incorrectly identiﬁed which elongates the time between
G0 and G1. Additionally, costs are too high with a long lead time and too big batch order sizes.
Projects are therefore delayed with high cost overruns and a lower quality than ﬁrst speciﬁed
by customers. Insuﬃcient test guides for UX and error identiﬁcation guides are unsatisfactory.
Finally, the solution due to a long time delay, can seem as mediocre in the market, according to
some customers. Alongside this, a time delay of poor part speciﬁcations or reuse of components
are not ideal for the project.
Veriﬁcation
A link between validation and veriﬁcation is often missed at G2.
Furthermore, an absolute
path instead of a relative path is followed which can lead to a bias of success and optimisation
uniqueness. The test sequence in G3 is often too late which can lead to a variation of instruments.
The tests are often ready far too late within the project because of an unclear scope of ﬁeld
trials. A lack of owned instruments within FOSS results in many re-runs of tests and a time
delay. Moreover, much the same as the supply category, insuﬃcient test guides for UX and
error identiﬁcation guides are unsatisfactory.
Normally between G1 and G2, clariﬁcation of
legal requirements is recommended but this is often too late. Aforementioned reuse of materials
within the technical and design functionality can cause a legal risk. Also, there is often no
compliance follow up on running production solution and parts.
5.2.4
Review the Results of the Workshop
Although the stakeholders remained the same from the interviews, answers and opinions changed
comparatively. This could have been because of the diﬀerent categories chosen or the fact that it
was conducted under particular circumstances compared to the interviews. The main outcomes
were that their was a distinctive lack of understanding at the start of projects according to
multiple stakeholders. This, within numerous departments can lead to the customer not being
involved because the stakeholders are unsure or unaware of the customer requirements. Another
major outcome of the workshop was the accumulation of minors throughout a project. This
is because as of now, there is no system to ensure the minors are correctly dealt with. Also,
much like the interviews, G2 seems to hold up departments and the overall project according to
collective stakeholders. This is mainly because of a lack of an assembly sequence, problems within
the prototype, and quality of materials from suppliers. Finally, a link between validation and
requirements is often missed. The requirements are often set towards the beginning of the project
but validating and documenting whether these were correctly implemented is missing often. The
objective of linking the validation to the requirements is to certify that the requirements are:
52
==PAGE:63==
complete, consistent, requirements do not conﬂict, does not contain technical errors, the needs
are not ambiguous, etc. If these are not checked, which seems to happen often in the testing
phase of FOSS, then problems associated with customer needs are missed.
5.2.5
Limitations of the Workshop
Much the same as the interviews, there are a number of limitations within the workshop. Firstly,
the stakeholders who were involved, had a vast range of diﬀerential knowledge. This meant
that everyone had their own opinion on the main root-cause and problems within FOSS. This
also leads to ”blind following” - where more conﬁdent stakeholders voice their opinions and
the less conﬁdent stakeholders will ”follow the pack”. Hence, potential incorrect knowledge and
inaccuracy can occur. Secondly, the understanding of questions and the workshop in general can
lead to diﬀerent opinions and a less in-depth analysis being conducted. Again, an understanding
of both Danish and English aided the researches. Finally, transparency between stakeholders is
extremely diﬃcult. Many stakeholders found it challenging to criticise their own department.
This is because it is easier to pass the blame on then to accept it. Therefore, it was decided not
to use names throughout the workshop analysis so reputations can be upheld.
5.3
Root Cause Analysis
In order to review the results from both the interviews and the workshop, a root-cause analysis
(RCA) section was created. This allows the results to be reviewed and analysed within ﬁgures.
Foremost, it allows a connection between both the Current Situation at FOSS (Section 4) and
the Analysis of the Interviews and Workshop (Section 5) to the Solution Development (Section
6). It begins with a relationship matrix diagram (Explained in Section 2.2.5) between the gates
and the technical terminology. This is essential to ﬁnd exactly which gate needs to be focused
on and which root-causes within this gate are the most problematic. This leads onto an adapted
Ishikawa Diagram (Explained in Section 2.2.6) where the same categories found in Atlas.ti are
used (Analysing the Content - Section 5.1.3) and combined with the workshop. This can then
be followed by a GUT diagram (Explained in Section 2.2.7) which quantiﬁes the interviews to
ﬁnd a solidiﬁed root-cause to be solved.
The RCA is one way of conducting a strong analysis but others were considered such as
the Monte Carlo Simulation. This tenable method is used in a complex environment where
uncertainty is higher. This and other methods were however disregarded due to their potentially
misleading errors and them extremely data-intensive. Therefore, the root-cause analysis of a
Ishikawa diagram followed by a GUT matrix was used.
5.3.1
Relationship Matrix Diagram
The beginning of the RCA is a Relationship Matrix Diagram (RMD). The RMD is a simple
checklist that shows how the gates within the stage gate model are related to the interviews
and workshop. To see how RMD is created and used, methodology section 2.2.5 was created.
53
==PAGE:64==
The RMD was completed with the aid of multiple stakeholders within FOSS and ﬁgure 27 was
created.
Figure 27: Relationship Matrix Diagram Completed with Stakeholder Input
The RMD is essential to scope down to only focusing on one gate. It is clear that gate 2 has
the largest proportion of correlated characteristics. Alongside this, the interaction roof of the
RMD represents an important relationship between the technical words and how they overlap.
Project Management has a strong relationship to all the technical terminology. This reiterates
the literature review of the risk management (Section 3.2).
There are certain limitations of using the RMD to correlate between the gates and the
technical terminology. The ﬁrst is the objective opinion given by multiple stakeholders. There
were many opinions that had to be taken into consideration. This was aided by using the contact
and averaging the scores of the other stakeholders. Another limitation is the RMD is that it has
to be combined with other root-cause analysis tools to ﬁnd problems.
Overall, the RCA will focus only on gate 2 due to its high total score of the relationship
to the technical terminology.
For more information on why this was chosen, the interviews
and workshop sections were essential. This lead onto an Ishikawa Diagram where each of the
technical characteristics are placed into a ﬁgure.
54
==PAGE:65==
5.3.2
Ishikawa Diagram
It is important to scope down to speciﬁc root-cause(s) and elucidate within the Solution Devel-
opment (Section 6). To do this, an Ishikawa diagram was adapted to see which were the most
proliﬁc reasons to be found. It is important to note that the Ishikawa is not Mutually Exclu-
sive Collectively Exhaustive (MECE) because some categories overlap. Hence, some important
aspects of the root-cause will be missed if the Ishikawa is MECE. Because of this overlapping,
the ﬁve original categories that were found in Atlas.ti were chosen. The deﬁnitions of the ﬁve
categories can be seen on Gathering the Information within the interview analysis (Section 5.1).
The Ishikawa diagram can be seen on ﬁgure 28.
Figure 28: Ishikawa Analysis taken from Multiple Interviews
All of the above mentioned possible causes are taken from the interviews or workshops. A
larger image showing the same Ishikawa has been placed in the Appendix (Section 9.6). The
Ishikawa diagram allows an overall portrayal of the stakeholders opinions onto a clear ﬁgure.
However, there are far too many causes of a high risk in project delays to ﬁx. Therefore, to
decipher which root-cause(s) to ﬁnd a solution for, the primary causes of each defect can be
quantiﬁed using a GUT analysis.
5.3.3
Gravity, Urgency, Tendency (GUT) Matrix
The most critical root-cause(s) cannot be found from a the qualitative triangulation analysis but
all possible risks of delays can. Therefore, the GUT was used. This quantiﬁes the claims made
by the original 7 stakeholders and places them into a table. Henceforth, the most essential and
inﬂuential root-cause(s) can be solved within the Solution Development. All the stakeholders
were handed a blank template of the GUT below (An example of this can be seen in Section
2.2.7. The terms of Gravity, Urgency and Tendency were explained alongside how the scale of 1
to 5 is deﬁned. Thereafter, the scores were averaged and rounded up or down then placed into
the table. All the problems are taken from the Ishikawa diagram which were in turn taken from
55
==PAGE:66==
either the interview or the workshop during the analysis of the interviews and workshop phase.
Figure 29 shows the outcome of the GUT table.
Figure 29: GUT Table Representing the Final Results
56
==PAGE:67==
Figure 29 was the aftermath of the Analysis of the interviews and workshop (Section 5). It
represents the qualitative stakeholder perception of the major root-causes that need to be ﬁxed
and the chronological order of this. If more time was permitted, then a longer and more in
depth triangulation method could have been used such as introducing past projects.
In order to see the opinions and how this relates to the root-cause analysis, the aforemen-
tioned section of Analysing the Content (Section 5.1.3) is essential. The broad range of opinions
within the Analysis of the interviews and workshop has allowed for a comprehensive root-cause
analysis. The GUT Matrix has three clear problems that must be addressed. These are listed
below:
1. Features are neglected on projects
Customers during G0 select requirements and features that are essential for their overall
satisfaction.
The root-cause analysis showed that in projects, features speciﬁcally are
lacking from the original talks. Thus, customers and UX often do not receive the aspired
features.
2. Unchecked changes in requirements
Requirements are often changed or deleted throughout a project. Due to a lack of docu-
mentation, requirements that are set by both FOSS and customers alike, ”go missing”.
3. Unknown unknowns causing high risk in projects
Because of a lack of risk management in general, projects are often put on hold due to
unknown unknowns. Although they cannot often be predicted, they can be mitigated in
numerous ways. However, at the present time, nothing is being done in this respect.
By seeking to solve the three largest root-causes according to important stakeholders, the un-
derlying processes can be ﬁxed. Henceforth, a solution can be found that can trace back to the
original activities and solve the primary cause of the problems. Once solved, issues with time
delays and over budget projects can be mitigated and reduced.
From the three major root-causes, it is important to note that these are human-based causes
(and not physical or organisational issues) because this will scope down the approach of the
Solution Development. Human causes within this project is deﬁned as: ”Stakeholders within a
project did something wrong or forgot to complete something that was needed.” This often leads
to physical causes such as incorrect prototypes and design errors. On the other hand, although
organisational causes are not directly linked to these three root-causes, the responsibility should
not rely on project managers but rather a process which is not correctly followed. By identifying
that the root-cause is human-based, it allows for a correct solution to be implemented within
the next section.
57
==PAGE:68==
6
Development of a Proposed Solution to Manage the Risk of
Delays at FOSS
Prior to this section, the current situation at FOSS and an analysis of 7 interviews and a
workshop were conducted. This lead to three primary root-causes that will be addressed. The aim
of the solution development is to evaluate baseline requirements and select the best solution to ﬁx
the root-causes. Alternative solutions and limitations of the chosen solution will be consolidated
into implementation an timeline and a cost-beneﬁt analysis. Finally, a validation of the solution
will be presented.
6.1
Overview of the Solution Development
The approach of ﬁnding and selecting a proposed solution is shown in ﬁgure 30.
It begins
with baseline requirements set by the Thesis and stakeholders alike.
This is followed by 3
alternative solutions. An quantitative evaluation can then be select 1 proposed solution. This
can be described in more detail through a timeline and cost-beneﬁt analysis. Finally, quotes of
limitations and beneﬁts of the proposed solution are shown in the validation section.
Figure 30: Solution Development Steps Taken
6.2
Baseline Requirements of the Solution
There are 8 baseline requirements. The ﬁrst 4 baseline requirements were requested from FOSS
stakeholders to ensure the solution is easy to implement into the current streams. The following 3
baseline requirements are from the root-cause analysis in the previous section (Section 5.3). The
ﬁnal baseline requirement was from the contact at FOSS to qualitatively analyse the proposed
solution.
The ﬁrst baseline requirement is to ensure the solution is stakeholder friendly to minimise
rework and adverse impacts of change. Alongside this baseline, the stakeholders need to under-
stand why the solution is put in place and how it will aid their projects. This is, as explained in
the current Situation at FOSS (Section 4), many tools and solutions have been suggested before,
58
==PAGE:69==
but within a few months, stakeholders have lost interest or not seen the applicability in projects.
Hence, the chosen solution must be explained and implemented in a manner that is understand-
able. This leads onto the third baseline requirement where the implementation timeline must
be realistic. The objectives, needs and goals of the solution have to be uniquely distinguishable
and a smooth transition in all projects. The fourth baseline is to align the system-of-interest
(the current solution) with the solution chosen. In simpler terms, this means that the Level 2
template that is currently being put in place does not hamper or obstruct the solution.
Alongside the essential baseline requirements given by FOSS stakeholders, the three root-
causes need to be mitigated within the proposed solution. These are seen on baseline requirement
5, 6 and 7. Henceforth, the solution must ensure features are not neglected within projects;
requirements set at the start of projects are not overlooked; and unknown unknowns are clariﬁed
and ﬁxed earlier within projects.
The baseline requirements explained above, are put in a
chronological order below:
1. Stakeholder friendly and simple solution
2. Ensure stakeholders understand the the expectation of the solution
3. Ensure the solution has a feasible approach in terms of objectives, needs and goals
4. Align the system-of-interest in terms of the current situation at FOSS
5. Ensure features are not neglected
6. Requirements set at the start of the project are checked throughout the project
7. High risk unknown unknowns are mitigated faster
8. Ensure the 3 swimlanes of FOSS are adhered to
The ﬁnal baseline requirement is a suggestion from the FOSS contact to ensure that a 3
swimlane approach is used. This, in simpler terms, means that people, process and tool are
considered qualitatively with the selection of any solutions. Therefore, after the baselines are
quantitatively examined, a qualitative explanation of the swimlanes will be presented.
6.3
Alternative Solutions
During this subsection, three alternative solutions will be represented. To start with, an expla-
nation of the solution framework will be described. Following this, a ﬁgure representing how the
solution will physically look, will be shown. Thereafter, the stakeholders who will be aﬀected
are described. Following this, the major beneﬁts of the solution are represented. Thereafter,
when, where and why it will happen at FOSS. It is important to note that the limitations to
each solution will not be fully addressed as this will restrict FOSS’ stakeholders from choosing
a certain solution.
59
==PAGE:70==
6.3.1
Solution 1 - Risk Management Statistical Analysis
The solution revolves around adding to the Azure DevOps initiation that is being trial tested
starting from August 2020 on a project called Dexter.
Before stating the solution, a brief
explanation on how Azure DevOps is essential for projects at FOSS. Azure DevOps is a Software
as a service (SaaS) platform created by Microsoft to provide an end-to-end tool-chain. FOSS
are potentially going to use Azure DevOps for each project to ensure that every aspect of a
project is documented. This will ensure that speciﬁcations set by customers are not deleted and
requirements set by FOSS are upheld. If not then each step of the project can be examined
for who is responsible and the stakeholder can be held accountable. Figure 31 below shows the
multiple aspects that can be used on Azure DevOps:
Figure 31: The Five Aspects of Azure DevOps
Solution 1 aims to be an addition to the Azure DevOps solution. More speciﬁcally, it will be
implemented within the Azure Test Plans aspect of Azure DevOps to statistically ﬁnd the most
reoccurring and common risks. At each project gate, currently, potential risks are not listed nor
checked on during a project. During this solution, at each gate, the project manager alongside
the project team will list down any potential risks. These potential risks will then be relayed and
kept in a Azure DevOps repository. Previous projects can then use this repository to ﬁnd if a
similar or comparable risk has been seen before. If there has been a comparable risk on another
project then the stakeholder who overcame the risk can be found. The project manager will
be able to look at these risks at each project team meeting and ﬁnd a mitigation plan. Azure
DevOps can also be used as an internal communication platform to enhance communication
between departments. Thus, reducing project delays (See 3.1 for more details).
It is important to note that this solution does not use risk management in order to ﬁnd a risk,
but a probabilistic approach to ﬁnd reoccurring problems at certain gates, of which stakeholders
can mitigate through diﬀerent techniques. This is a proven approach of ﬁnding speciﬁc risks and
mitigating them individually according to the literature (Described in more detail on Section
3.2.2). An example of how this will work can be seen on ﬁgure 32. This shows speciﬁcally gate
60
==PAGE:71==
2 and the major 5 problems that could occur on a project, in a simple pie chart.
Figure 32: Solution 1 Statistical Analysis, An Example Showing Five Potential Risks at Gate 2
Who will be aﬀected?
This solution aims at aﬀecting all project managers throughout multiple projects. As explained
in the description above, the statistical analysis will be used to mitigate the largest risks in
projects.
This will therefore aﬀect mainly the project managers with their approach to the
project. But non-directly, this will also eﬀect the project team.
What are the potential beneﬁts?
After talking to the stakeholders about this solution, these are the potential beneﬁts that we
agree on:
1. No added tools needed so it is a simple addition the solution being added to Azure DevOps
2. Continuous integration in all projects
3. Open platform that each stakeholder can access
61
==PAGE:72==
4. Easy to manage and implement new risk mitigation techniques when a project manager
sees necessary
5. Easy to ﬁnd the most proliﬁc risk at each gate on each project
When will it take place?
Because Azure DevOps is going live during August 2020, this solution can be put in place
without an implementation timeline. This means that it can be tested at the same time as the
Azure DevOps implementation.
Where will it be put in place?
Azure DevOps is a SaaS platform so it will all be online. This has a major limitation in my
opinion as not physically being able to see a copy, might hinder its importance to a project
team.
Why will it happen?
This solution will not only help stakeholders keep track of their risks within a project but also use
past projects as a risk mitigation technique. This is because old documentation and approaches
can be used to base project timelines oﬀ, not just a gut instinct. Hence, this solution will give a
statistical analysis of a risk at a gate, what the frequency of it occurring on projects is and the
previous technique to mitigate it from occurring.
6.3.2
Solution 2 - Creation of a Risk Management Tool
The second solution does not base itself on the level 2 template, nor the Azure DevOps (as
solution 1 entails), rather it uses a combination of the Delphi Method and a Red, Amber, Green
(RAG) rating. The Delphi Method is a process forecasting tool that uses multiple questions
to various members of a project team (mostly the project manager) to better aggregate the
opinions and risks of each element in a project. It will have three rounds of questions starting at
the top tier of scope (all stakeholders involved in the project) and ending with only the project
managers.
Hence, a better timeline can be created.
Alongside this, an integration of RAG
ratings will ﬁnd whether each step of the process will be at high risk to becoming a project time
delay.
Six categories will be chosen for the questions are based on the literature review of stage
fates (Section 3.4). According to Cooper, the 6 categories below, once optimised, will lead to
an improved project timeline. The 6 categories are as follow:
−Strategic ﬁt & Importance
−Product and Competitive advantage
−Market attractiveness
62
==PAGE:73==
−Synergies and Core competencies
−Manufacturing and technical feasibility
−Financial Risk vs Reward
To see how this will work in stage one of the questions (the highest scope), ﬁgure 33 shows
an example of gate 2 and questions that the project team could potentially be asked:
Figure 33: Solution 2 - Quantitative Methodology, An Example Showing the First Iteration at
Gate 2
The ﬁrst key area in ﬁgure 33 is the Criteria / Questions. Because this is only the ﬁrst set
of questions out of three, these questions are generalised so that all stakeholders can answer.
The stakeholders then clearly mark from 0 - 10, where they personally answer each question. 0
and 10 are clearly marked but the boundary between them is ambiguous. This has purposely
been set this way in order to have an honest and authentic stance. Afterwards, the score can be
tallied up. If multiple stakeholders have answered red for the same questions and a pattern can
be seen from the project manager, then there is a high risk. One addition to this solution is the
ﬁnal line of ”STAKEHOLDER RECOMMENDATION ON G2”. This was added to ﬁnd out
whether they should continue to gate 2 or there is previously unﬁnished and high risk problems
at the end of G1. Hence, there are four tick boxes. The ﬁrst is GO which means there is a low
risk of anything delaying a project. The next is KILL where the stakeholder thinks the project
needs to be stopped immediately. The third is RECYCLE where G1’s input is insuﬃcient, but
the project still has potential to yield beneﬁts. Finally is the HOLD where the stakeholder
believes the project should relapse until a high risk problem is ﬁxed.
Who will be aﬀected?
The project manager will have the most say in regards to the Delphi Method because they
have the most experience. Alongside this, all relevant stakeholders will be asked to ﬁll in the
questionnaire (project team, steering committee, customers, etc.) before the project oﬃcially
commences (G0).
63
==PAGE:74==
What are the potential beneﬁts?
After talking to the stakeholders about this solution, these are the potential beneﬁts that we
agree on:
1. Better time planning at each gate (Less time delays)
2. Speed and quality of execution of innovative products improved
3. Fast and ﬂexible at each gate the Delphi approach is used
4. More customer driven than previous process templates
5. Unknown unknowns can be dealt with faster due to a larger internal communication inte-
gration of stakeholders
When will it take place?
This solution can be implemented onto a new project within the next months.
It needs to
be explained and trained into project managers and project teams alike but overall is a fast
implementation option.
Where will it be put in place?
The solution will be both online and printed out. This will ensure the project managers can
learn the process in and out whilst the project team can visibly see the questions next to the
stage gate model.
Why will it happen?
This solution aims at solving multiple root-causes that were found. Firstly, the project managers
can use the same approach and discuss why they have set a certain deadline on a project.
Secondly, the VoC will be implemented and utilised more eﬃciently on each project. Thirdly,
the speciﬁcations set by customers and the veriﬁcation barriers set by project teams can be
documented and questioned within the Delphi Approach. Finally, the quality of products should
be increased from asking appropriate questions to the streams.
6.3.3
Solution 3 - Organisational Learning
The third solution aligned both the interest of FOSS and one of the root-cause from the Ishikawa
diagram. This however instead of being on a project speciﬁc level, is a portfolio level. Because
of diﬀerentiating approaches from project managers (as explained in Section 5.3.2), timeline
plans are created by the PM alone. This has caused many projects to be delayed as often, the
prototyping, manufacturing and producing of the end product does not include the original PM.
To avoid this, the third solution includes an organisational learning theme. Because there is
64
==PAGE:75==
a huge barrier currently at FOSS between competing initiatives (as explained in section 4.2),
resources are not clearly aligned or prioritised based.
Solution 3 uses a project prioritisation to clearly outline where essential resources are needed.
This can be updated and changed depending on the project management and the steering
committee. Figure 34 represents how this will work.
Figure 34: Solution 3 - Portfolio Prioritization, An Example Showing the Overview of all Projects
at FOSS and their Priorities
On the left-hand side of ﬁgure 34, the current projects are listed (A to M). Thereafter, in
chronological order is the tier of how important the project is. The ﬁnal 4 columns are fact based
frequency and organisational eﬀect quantitative numbering. The ﬁrst of the four is Net Present
Value (NPV), which is simply the diﬀerence between the new outﬂow costs and the estimated
income proﬁt. The next column is the ease, which is estimated by the project manager. The
third of the four columns is the amount of project hours estimated and ﬁnally, the fourth column
is the testing hours required to ﬁnish the product.
The top right key shows an on-going colour scheme where capability constraints, capacity
constraints or a project being on hold can be seen. Thus, resources can be allocated better
between projects.
Who will be aﬀected?
Project managers from all projects as well as the steering committee. The project team and
outside stakeholders will not be eﬀected within this solution.
What are the potential beneﬁts?
After talking to the stakeholders about this solution, these are the potential beneﬁts that we
agree on:
65
==PAGE:76==
1. Better resource planning over all projects
2. Increased speed of prioritised projects
3. Overview of all projects onto one ﬁgure
4. Easily adaptable approach to better allocate human resources with a reason
When will it take place?
This solution will take a longer period of time due to numerous stakeholders being involved. It
will also be a long and tedious discussion between the steering group and the project managers.
In order to overcome this, I advise for this solution to have a meeting per month to discuss
changes that may have occurred on speciﬁc projects.
Where will it be put in place?
This solution will be both a physical copy hung up in the project room as well as an easy online
version. No changes should be made without informing both the steering committee and the
project managers it will eﬀect.
Why will it happen?
As discussed numerous times within the root-cause analysis, their is a major problem at FOSS
with PM and their approaches to projects. Thus, this allocation of resources will allow a rigid
approach for selecting which project should be prioritised and why. Moreover, as projects become
more complicated throughout the stage gate model, more time has to be allocated to testing and
prototyping. Thus, if projects have streamlined their timelines via the organisational learning
table then project teams can align themselves more.

As the table implies, solution 2 is the best suited according to the baseline solutions but
