To show how each
swimlane relates to one another, ﬁgure 36 represents the alignment:
Figure 36: The 3 Swimlanes and How They Need to Align to the Proposed Solution
67
==PAGE:78==
Solution 1 aims at changing the process that the project follows. Hence, the stakeholders will
have a strong focus on why a certain outcome has occurred. In terms of the tool, as it is only a
basic statistical analysis, a new tool will not be added but there will be large emphasis on the
interaction points between the stakeholder and the risks at certain stages of a gate. The people
area of this solution will be detailed with a step-by-step guide of how a stakeholder will interact
with the current procedures. This will allow for an alignment between the Azure.DevOps im-
plementation and the people who will be using solution 1.
Solution 2 is speciﬁcally aimed at gate 2 and adding in a tool. The solution changes the
process at the start of a project by involving multiple stakeholders to analyse numerous risks.
Thus, the stakeholder process focuses on the what, when and why outcomes. The tool aspect
is the largest of solution 2 and henceforth, it is important that the system design is pragmatic
whilst being eﬀective. The functional requirements of the tool must be aligned with the proce-
dure activities. The people aspect again will have a step-by-step guideline of how stakeholders
will use the tool and more speciﬁcally, a detailed timeline of how long it will take to implement
into FOSS.
Solution 3 is another tool based solution. The process of choosing resources for a project
is the aim of this solution so the focus point is on why and when. In terms of the tool, the
emphasis is on a basic but structured utensil. This will make sure that the functional require-
ments are satisﬁed. To align itself with the people, the tool must have a priority on including all
project managers. The activities in which solution 3 requires are simple to establish a greater
cost-beneﬁt for the stakeholders.
In conclusion to the evaluate and select section, solution 2 is the proposed solution that the
ﬁnal solution will be based around. Having said this, it is important to include certain aspects of
the remaining two solutions. From solution 1, it is essential to have a strong focus on including
all relevant stakeholders. This will ensure that the ”people” element of the swimlanes is followed
suﬃciently. From solution 3, it is imperative to include a resource aspect within the proposed
solution. This will establish a more relevant tool for the project managers and fulﬁll a more
in depth ”tool” aspect of the swimlanes. From this, the proposed solution can now be fully
described.

==PAGE:79==
6.5
Proposed Solution
The previous section demonstrated through a qualitative and quantitative analysis, that the
proposed solution will be based around solution 2. This solution, as explained in the Alternative
Solutions (Section 6.3) consists of three iterations. How this solution will work will be explained
throughout this segment. Before starting, it is important to note that 3 meetings will be held
to explain the solution to the stakeholders, as well as examples of how each iteration will work
alongside their project (Explained in more detail on Section 6.6). After the proposed solution
is described, a timeline and a cost-beneﬁt can be created.
Iteration 1
The ﬁrst iteration is aimed at all relevant stakeholders. This includes the project managers,
project team, steering committee, relevant streams with all colleagues and customers when and
if needed. The ﬁrst iteration with its respected questions is the most inﬂuential for the project
manager to decipher a more accurate timeline of the project. Hence, iteration 1 has the most in
depth and concentrated questions. It is also important to mention that this solution is aimed
at speciﬁcally gate 2 so the questions are proportional to that gate. Figure 37 represents the
questions that will be handed to all the stakeholders before the gate 2 timeline has been set:
Figure 37: First Iteration Questions for Gate 2 only
Iteration 1 consists of 12 questions with 6 categories.
As explained prior to this, these
categories were chosen based on the stage gate model literature review (Section 3.4.1).
12
questions are asked within this iteration in order to justify how conﬁdent the stakeholders are
69
==PAGE:80==
about the current project. A short explanation of what a 0 answer for a question is and what a
10 answer is. After a stakeholder has ﬁlled in the template, a total score can be totalled in the
bottom right corner.
−A score of ≤60 is in the red zone
−A score between 61 −84 is the orange zone
−A score of ≥85 is in the green zone
The total scores given above are based on normative frequency data between 0 - 10. If the
total score of multiple stakeholders are in the red zone, then the project manager must look
into why this has happened and how this can be corrected. The ﬁnal note for iteration 1 is
the Stakeholder recommendation on G2: on the bottom left of ﬁgure 37. It shows how the
stakeholder dictates the gate before starting and their feeling on the current situation.
Although weights and biases for stakeholders are not used, the project manager can decide
which stakeholders have more experience and therefore more knowledge about the questions used.
This could be an addition if the project manager sees ﬁt for this. The project manager is also
responsible for sending out the questionnaire as well as ensuring all the relevant stakeholders have
ﬁlled the iteration in. After all relevant stakeholders have completed the template, the project
manager uses the questions to dictate how conﬁdent the departments are about the current
project timeline for gate 2 (Set at the beginning of the project). If conﬁdence for completing is
high and the majority of stakeholders are within the green zone then iteration 2 and 3 can be
skipped. If not, then iteration 2 can begin.
Iteration 2
This iteration consists of only the project manager, steering committee and the project team.
The second iteration should only be used if the ﬁrst iteration is in the red/orange zone or if the
project manager sees ﬁt. The second iteration consists of similar questions to the ﬁrst iteration
but it conducted as a brainstorming activity. The questions which are listed in ﬁgure 38 are the
basis for this brainstorming activity:
Figure 38: Second Iteration Questions for Gate 2 only
70
==PAGE:81==
The questions are based on the same 6 aforementioned categories but with intellectual nour-
ishment. This essentially means that the questions are more diﬃcult to answer with a straight
forward number and should be discussed during the workshop. The brainstorming activity will
consist of the project manager, the steering committee and the project team (the timeline will be
discussed in the next section). It will be a 3 hour workshop where 30 minutes will be allocated
to answer and solve the required problems of each question. If more time is required then time
will allocated before and after the 3 hours. Iteration 1’s ﬁlled out questions will also be present
to answer substantial risks that may occur in G2. Iteration 2 is important as it bridges the gap
between the risk management of time delays within G2 and how they can be resolved. It is also
the basis for the third and ﬁnal iteration, number 3.
Iteration 3
This iteration consists of only the project manager and is a list of questions that should be
answered before G2 starts. As mentioned prior to this, this should only be a last ditch eﬀort
if iteration 1 and 2 ended in unresolved risks.
Unlike the two prior iterations, iteration 3’s
template does not have a score, but should be answered qualitatively by the project manager
alone. Thus, all stakeholders can observe how the project manager has set the timeline and
whether it is realistic. Figure 39 represents iteration 3:
Figure 39: Third Iteration Questions for Gate 2 only
71
==PAGE:82==
16 questions were created in iteration 3. These questions again are based on the original 6
categories with an extra four questions based on the previous iterations. The concept of these
questions is that the project manager can physically show how the previous iterations have been
incorporated into the new timeline. Although some of the questions that are listed are diﬃcult
for the project manager to answer, it allows a precise risk assessment of where project delays
might start.
In order to see the timeline for training the stakeholders and the extra time needed for the
solution to be added for a project, implementation timeline.
6.6
Implementation Timeline
There are two aspects to the timeline; the ﬁrst is the training time needed for the stakeholders
to understand the proposed solution and how it will be used at FOSS and the second is how
much extra time the proposed solution takes up on a project. The times are estimated and may
vary once fully implemented. Both the researcher and the contact have estimated the times
collectively.
6.7
Training Timeline
For stakeholders to grasp the importance of the 3 iterations and how they are used, a training
timeline must be created. This will occur over 3 training days consisting of 4 hours for each
workshop. The time span has been given as 1 quarter which is 3 months. This allows new
projects starting in January to implement the said solution. Figure 40 shows how the training
days will be divided for best eﬃciency:
Figure 40: Training Timeline for the Proposed Solution
72
==PAGE:83==
1st Meeting
The ﬁrst meeting subsists of all project managers. The idea of this is to explain and demon-
strate how the 3 iterations will work. This includes how the questions are laid out; when it will
be used; how the questions are sent out; who is included in each iteration; when the questions
should be used; etc. The decisions about which projects should start with the proposed solution
will be made as well as an overall explanation on why this solution will aid the project timeline.
Basic Preparations
In-between the 1st and 2nd meetings, the project managers are expected to make some basic
preparations in their spare time. A set amount of time has not been given to this but it is ex-
pected to take between 4 - 8 hours across the span of a few weeks. Within this preparation, the
3 iterations will be sent round and the project managers are expected to introduce this to their
respected project teams. Alongside this, questions and discussions will be met and answered by
the project managers who have been taught the iterations.
2nd Meeting
The second meeting is ensuring that the language the current tool makes sense to the stake-
holders and the project team. As explained during the interviews and workshop, it is important
that FOSS understand the technical terms or they will go amiss. A ﬁnal explanation on the 3
iterations will be made with a critical eye on whether the project managers can implement the
solution into a project eﬃciently.
Tool Brainstorms
In-between the 2nd and 3rd meeting are tool brainstorms. This is where all possible risks of
the tool failing are mentioned and mitigated. Although this solution goes into detail of how it
will work, it is important that once the tool is implemented and tested, the stakeholders make
a list of any problems. This may cause project managers to have individual meetings to assure
the project team about the solution. Open-source platforms should be used so project team
members can anonymously ask about any insecurities they have regarding the solution (These
will not be covered or addressed during this solution).
3rd Meeting
The 3rd and ﬁnal meeting should have a reverse-classroom feel.
This is when some of the
project managers explain the proposed solution to the project team and steering committee.
This ensures that they as well as the others know the proposed solution extremely well. Any
ﬁnal questions or remarks will also be explained in detail during this meeting.

interviews with proposed stakeholders, a cost-beneﬁt analysis can be created.
6.9
Cost-Beneﬁt Analysis
It is important that a solution has a cost-beneﬁt analysis (CBA) to compare with a non-existent
solution. This is a systematic approach for estimating the strengths and weaknesses in terms of
cost for choosing this solution or not. The calculation can be given as:
Recommended Hours * Number of Stakeholders * Hourly Wage
The CBA uses the extra project hours timeline to express in monetary terms how much cost
FOSS will use prior to initiating the solution and the cost for every project thereafter. A table
has been created in ﬁgure 42 to show how much extra time is needed per stakeholder and the
overall estimated beneﬁts:
74
==PAGE:85==
Figure 42: The Cost-Beneﬁt Analysis Table of the Proposed Solution
As it was not permitted to have the wages of each worker, Danish average wages were taken.
The estimated hours of each sector was discussed with stakeholders in order to assure a relative
assessment could be created. The ﬁrst part of the ﬁgure is the initial implementation costs. This
is a one-oﬀcost that FOSS endure to ensure the tool is fully learnt. It will cost approximately
176,688 DKK. This will increase gradually as more employees join FOSS and will need to learn
the same process.
The second part is the project implementation costs. This is how much every project costs to
run the proposed solution. This is approximately 94,371 DKK.
The third part to the table is the beneﬁts. These are extremely diﬃcult to calculate as the
proposed solution has not been implemented so this was completed by using stakeholders and
online research of similar tools being implemented. The total beneﬁts for every solution (not
including the original implementation costs) is approximately 655,629 DKK.

Validation of the Proposed Solution
The validation section considers the BABOK Knowledge Area Solution Assessment and Vali-

to ensure it meets the companies needs and implementation expectations. The two areas the
validation will look at are: Assess Proposed Solution and Evaluate Solution Performance.

Assess Proposed Solution
According to the BABOK, this section has a purpose of ”assessing proposed solutions in order to
determine how closely they meet stakeholder and solution requirements.” To do so, stakeholders
from FOSS were asked for their opinions and these will be quoted below. Names will not be
used, but rather their position or department in the company.
The stakeholder in charge of initiative 5 within FOSS said:
”I see the solution as very interesting because it is based on dozens of in-depth interviews in
FOSS, which then subsequently hones in on central root-causes and solutions - based on facts.
FOSS R&D is in general lacking eﬀective tools to help the project manager. Solutions like this
will surely be interesting to test, and if eﬀective, then integrated into our process ﬂows. For the
project managers who have been at FOSS for many years any change in their way of working
will by nature be a challenge. The beneﬁts will probably be most evident for projects managers
with less tenure.”
The stakeholder who is head of business excellence:
”I think the solution will be a challenge for the project managers speciﬁcally. They are not used
to implementing reliable tools. However, I hope that this tool will be used because it has a solid
root-cause analysis behind it. Before this, multiple tools were tested but these were not backed
up with any data. The beneﬁts I see, as shown within the report, will be a more in-depth project
timeline, compared to what we have now. I also think the tool will be adapted a little to better
ﬁt some projects but think this will be shown more in future projects.”
A project manager said:
”The solution fulﬁlls the requirements we (Project Managers) set but it will also be challenging
for everyone to get on board. I think after the 3 workshops planned then I will have a better idea
of how this will work. I think it is also important that the solution is updated for each project
instead of just leaving it. The major beneﬁt I see is a systematic approach to project timelines,
instead of relying mainly on myself and other project managers only.”

==PAGE:87==

6.10.2
Evaluate Solution Performance
Due to time constraints of the project (Mentioned during Section 7.3), the solution will not be put
into a project before Quarter 1 of 2021. It is however important to discuss the evaluation method
the solution. According to BABOK, this section aims to: “Evaluate functioning solutions to
understand the value they deliver and identify opportunities for improvement.” in order for this
to occur, the baseline requirements must be recalled and aligned with the proposed solution:
1. Stakeholder friendly and simple solution
2. Ensure stakeholders understand the the expectation of the solution
3. Ensure the solution has a feasible approach in terms of objectives, needs and goals
4. Align the system-of-interest in terms of the current situation at FOSS
5. Ensure features are not neglected
6. Requirements set at the start of the project are checked throughout the project
7. High risk unknown unknowns are mitigated faster
8. Ensure the 3 swimlanes of FOSS are adhered to
The 8 baseline requirements were set to ensure that the solutions met stakeholder expectations
while solving project delays. Once the solution is fully implemented, these categories should be
used in a qualitative manner. For example, number 4 is to align the system-of-interest in terms
of the current situation at FOSS. If the system-of-interest changes then some of the questions
on iteration 1 must also be changed. It is important to remain ﬂexible with the approach or
innovation might be impacted (As explained in literature Section 3.3).
It is also important to mention that a seminar will be held after the Thesis is handed in. This
will present all the results and ﬁndings to the R&D department and hand over responsibility for
continuing the implementation of the solution.

==PAGE:88==
7
Discussion
This section explores the underlying concept of the literature review and aligns this with the
proposed solution. It allows the reader to grasp the importance of the Thesis and contributes to
the overall research conducted. It begins with the overall ﬁndings of the multiple analysis’ and
how this aﬀected the solution development. Thereafter, a link to the literature review can be made
with consideration on how large of an impact the Thesis has created. Penultimately, the studies
limitations in regard to the tools, process and potential weaknesses. Finally, considerations for
future research with speciﬁc emphasis on important unanswered questions.
7.1
Findings within the Thesis
The objective of this Thesis was to analyse and explore FOSS to ﬁnd a root-cause for project
delays. The solution to this root-cause had to keep FOSS’s innovative aspect whilst improving
their risk of a potential project delay. The current literature (Section 3.3.1) established a pattern
of qualitative methodologies to optimise project delays such as Agile and Lean. These however
have had little impact on FOSS speciﬁcally. This is due to their Stage Gate Model approach
which as discussed in the literature review is a rigid Waterfall methodology. In order to main-
tain their current approach whilst mitigating risks, an investigation took place which utilised
7 interviews and a workshop. During these, multiple stakeholders shared identical opinions on
what caused the major project delays. The 5 main issues that were looked into where: Project
Management, Quality, Speciﬁcations, Time Restraints and Veriﬁcation. An unexpected out-
come of the Analysis of Interviews and Workshop (Section 5) was the honesty of all stakeholders
within their interviews. Many project managers confessed that their current methodologies were
outdated and possibly caused project delays on many of their recent projects. The ﬁndings in-
dicated that 3 major root-causes should be looked into whilst proposed a solution. These were:
1) Features given by customers at G0 are often missing or neglected throughout the project; 2)
Requirements set at the start of the project are not checked throughout the project; 3) High
risk unknown unknowns increase throughout a project. These ﬁndings were essential to ﬁgure
out a solution that will aid stakeholders but ensure FOSS’s innovative culture will remain. This
allowed myself to advance on the creating new improved methodological solutions which could
improve their project delays. 3 solutions which were created were rigorously analysed by the
stakeholders in order to choose which one would be selected. A proposed solution was based on
an evaluation of 8 baseline requirements set by FOSS and the root-cause analysis. The proposed
solution consisted of 3 iterations, all containing of a diﬀerent amount of stakeholders. The pro-
posed solution uses a quantitative methodological approach on iteration 1 and 2 to ﬁnd possible
risks at gate 2 of the Stage Gate Model. The 3rd iteration is an assurance from the project
manager to the other primary stakeholders that a project delay was mitigated. The validation
to this solution can be seen from quotes of stakeholders on Section 6.10.
78
==PAGE:89==
7.2
Link to Literature
It is important to link the Thesis to the Literature Review (Section 3). To avoid project delays,
literature suggests to use ﬁve criteria: internal communication; use of technology; key stakeholder
inﬂuences; market endorsement; and quality of products. The Thesis looks into in-particular the
internal communication and the key stakeholder inﬂuences. The internal communication was
kept high between the the researcher and the essential stakeholders at FOSS through interviews,
meetings and a workshop. This allowed an internal communication that utilised the wheel of
leadership through social dimensions. The second part of the Project Delays and How They
Can be Avoided was the use of key stakeholder inﬂuences. Literature suggests that by having
project managers on-board with the solution and accepting, the project team will also learn
faster. This cannot be proven during this Thesis as the solution has not been tested but should
be reﬂected upon once the solution has been fully implemented.
The Risk Management Literature Review (Section 3.2) deﬁned the terms used throughout
the Thesis.
Figure 15 linked the risk management reviews including the inputs, tools and
techniques, and the outputs. By using my solution, this table can be added to with a risk the 3
iteration solution. This can be seen on ﬁgure 43:
Figure 43: Addition to the Summary of inputs, tools and techniques and outputs on risk man-
agement plan phase (Mulder, 2018)
The input is stakeholder judgement and opinion on the iterations. This is seen through
all three iterations. The tool and technique is the methodology described on Section 6.5. The
output is a gate 2 project timeline; a quantitative stakeholder template for iteration 1; and ﬁnally
a risk template for iteration 3. This aids the current risk management outputs and templates
currently used in organisations.
Another link to literature was utilising the Challenges Facing Innovation Organisations (Sec-
tion 3.3). As described, unknown unknowns are a large aspect as it explains ”It is argued that
the more an organisation knows, the more uncertain it seems to be.” This is true amongst FOSS
and was one of the root-causes found for project delays (Section 5.3). One of the limitations to
this section was the lack of empirical data. Because my analysis was through interviews and a
79
==PAGE:90==
workshop, there is still a lack of empirical data for this subject. To overcome this, the literature
suggests the creation of speciﬁc risk management tools for an organisation. This was followed
and the solution, although ﬂexible enough to use in other organisations, is speciﬁcally for FOSS.
Within this section, 2 methodologies were looked into; Agile and Lean (Section 3.3). Although
literature tens to dictate that Waterfall and Agile approaches are contrasting (Important to
remember that FOSS are using the Stage Gate Model - AKA. Waterfall Approach), similarities
across this Thesis have been identiﬁed. The ﬁrst of which is the challenge of documentation
which FOSS struggled with before starting initiative 5. The second similarity is the time com-
mitment that is required to learn the methodological approaches if employees have not used it
before. Thirdly, is the complex common management communication misunderstanding. Much
of the Thesis looked into how project managers dictate the overall project timeline, henceforth
communication to the project team is essential. If this is lacking, then literature suggests that
a lower level of project performance will be upheld.
Within the Project Gate Literature Review (Section 3.4), a large proportion is dedicated to
How Project Gates Relate with Innovation (Section 3.4.1). This looks into the Four Pillars of
the Innovation Performance (Figure 17). It is important to reﬂect how this solution adapts to
the four pillars which is shown on ﬁgure 44 below:
Figure 44: How the Four Pillars of the Innovative Performance Framework ﬁt into the Thesis
As the the ﬁgure shows, the innovative strategy and the Stage Gate Model were both consid-
ered highly within the proposed solution. Unfortunately, the culture leadership and the portfolio
management were not. This was due to time restraints and the ambiguity of a large organisation.
This lead to the scope only being on the R&D and not on the overall culture, nor the portfolio
80
==PAGE:91==
management of this. The ﬁnal aspect to the literature review were the 6 categories based on
the four pillars:
−Strategic Fit
−Product and Competitive Advantage
−Market Attractiveness
−Technical Feasibility
−Synergies/Core Competencies
−Financial Risk vs. Reward
These 6 categories were slightly adapted for the proposed solution but were the main basis
for the 3 iterations. Cooper created these categories to ensure that project delays could be
avoided within the Stage Gate Model approach. Again, without fully testing the solution, this
cannot be proved.
7.3
Limitations of the Thesis
Study limitations are important to discuss as it may aﬀect future research on the same sub-
jects. Clearly acknowledging the studies constraints shows an awareness of potential weaknesses
throughout the Thesis. The ﬁrst limitation was the scope of the project. Due to time con-
straints, initiative 5 was chosen, speciﬁcally the R&D sector within FOSS. This meant that only
7 interviews were selected out of 31 because of the scope. This could be seen as a selection bias.
As the interviews were not conducted by the researcher directly, a misinterpretation of language
could be perceived.
As discussed in the literature link, there was a distinct lack of quantitative methodologies
used within the stage gate model.
This meant that a large limitation of this study was no
prior studies with the same theoretical background. This meant that an entirely new solution
development had to be created to identify and solve the gap in the prior literature.
Much like many studies, a distinct lack of time led to a less thoroughly triangulation than
ﬁrst intended. The study uses interviews and a workshop but to be comprehensive, historic
projects should have also been used. This could have led to either a more detailed root-cause
analysis which meticulously conformed with the research or contradicted the previously stated
solution.
The methodological approach this Thesis used was based on numerous tools and techniques.
Many of which could be questioned as to why these were used and not others. Simply, the
tools were, as explained earlier, selected based on prior research and to ensure that the correct
root-cause and proposed solution could be found (Also explained further in Section 2.2). If other
tools were used then it is plausible that diﬀerent root-causes could be found. This limitation was
addressed by stakeholders at FOSS as well as myself to ensure the best possible beneﬁts were
achieved from this Thesis. An important note to add to this, is that FOSS suggested certain
tools but these were critically assessed to ensure the quality of the Thesis remained high.
81
==PAGE:92==
7.4
Future Research
To overcome certain limitations, future research must be conducted which aids the current
study. As discussed in the limitations of the study, an area which must have future research is
the development of quantitative methodological approaches within the stage gate model. This
is because many gates are passed without thoroughly ﬁnishing, hence, critical paths seem to be
missing.
Expanding on the full cost-beneﬁt of using the proposed solution will aid to capture a more
precise outcome for organisations. A further explanation of this research will enhance the ability
for an organisation to enhance their involvement in projects.
In depth exploration of how to inﬂuence project teams agnostic or sceptical Project Managers
would be very helpful. Future research could compare how diﬀerent Project Managers use strate-
gies to implement the proposed solution with their own peer-inﬂuencing strategies. Research
could explore the leadership roles of collective leadership using the steering committee.
Finally, a relatively narrow aspect of the Thesis but importantly was the data collection.
Better methodological approaches could be endeavoured and researched into which stakeholders
have the most inﬂuential involvement in projects and how these eﬀect project teams. Hence,
triangulation of more intense interviews and workshops could aid the data collection.
82
==PAGE:93==
8
Conclusion
The initially stated overarching aim of this research was to identify and analyse FOSS in terms
of their R&D sector to ﬁnd root-causes of project delays and suggest a proposed solution. Whilst
recognising the limitations of the study, I believe that this has been achieved in a critical contex-
tual sense (Limitations discussed on Section 7.3). The beneﬁt of the Thesis is two-fold. The ﬁrst
being that a bottom-up approach ensured that FOSS can elaborate then re-use the analysis to
stabilise further improvements (Described throughout the Analysis of the Interviews and Work-
shop on Section 5). Whilst FOSS have analysed their performance numerous times, this was
often through stakeholders who are employed at the institute. This gives a large bias and tools
are often re-used instead of adopting and adapting revised tools. This Thesis used tools that
were largely foreign for FOSS but allowed an outstanding analysis. Henceforth, the bottom-up
approach integrated with accurate tools identiﬁed multiple root-causes for project delays.
The second is towards literature. As entrenched in the objective of the Thesis, a quantitative
solution was created to enhance the methodological approaches currently used within the Stage
Gate Model in a VUCA environment. Before this, literature was lacking in regard to quantitative
approaches used within the Stage Gate Model. Alongside this, using the 5 themes to improve in-
novative organisations in terms of project delays were described (Section 3.5). 2 out of 5 of these
themes were used: internal communication and key stakeholder inﬂuences to create the proposed
solution. Furthermore, the lack of previous research within globally leading organisations left for
a challenging investigation but through the use of FOSS, a proportion of this gap has been ﬁlled.
To represent the prosperous outcome, the problem formulation must be addressed with its sub-
questions.
Research Sub-Question 1: What tools have been executed by FOSS to eliminate the risk of
not completing project milestones?
As described in the Current Situation at FOSS (Section 4), FOSS initiated risk tools based on
the project at hand. This however was ﬂawed as it depended on the project manager and hence,
documentation was often missing. The Level 1 template (Figure 20) was then brought into FOSS
in order to solve this problem. However, over time, this became too simple for a complicated
project because of the multi-dimensional streams. Within initiative 5, the Level 2 template was
created (Figure 22). This is still being implemented but from the analysis of FOSS, there is an
implication that project documentation levels will increase which should decrease overall project
delays.
83
==PAGE:94==
Research Sub-Question 2: How have these tools been integrated into FOSS to reduce project
delays?
The previous tools were unfortunately not conducted as eﬃciently as possible. This is due to
many factors such as project management changing and the project team not cooperating as
proactively as ﬁrst hoped. The Level 2 template is still on trial so their are large diﬃculties on
analysing whether the performance will be successful.
Research Sub-Question 3: What are the supplementary tools that can be implemented to
reduce the risk of project delays at FOSS - and what are the beneﬁts/limitations to these?
The Delphi methodology was combined with a Red, Amber, Green approach to create a 1 -
10 quantitative solution. This tool aims at bridging the gap between the Stage Gate Model
and quantitative methodologies whilst improving the current project timeline estimations. The
limitations and beneﬁts were fully examined during Section 7.3.
From the above mentioned 3 sub-questions, it is now possible to answer the problem formulation.
Problem Formulation: How can a risk analysis be used to reduce project delays in an inno-
vative organisation?
The Thesis used a risk analysis on 7 interviews and a workshop. This found 3 root-causes of
project delays at FOSS: features are neglected on projects; unchecked changes in requirements;
and unknown unknowns causing high risks in projects. By using the 3 root-causes and baseline
requirements given by FOSS, a proposed solution was created. The aim was to reduce delays
through a proposed solution. The validation from stakeholders suggested that this was plausible
but without a full implementation into a project, this cannot be proved.
It is important to note that this solution has not been fully tested, so the validation of the
solution performance should be reﬂected upon after this is complete (Section 6.10.2).
